You are a document structure analyst specializing in educational textbooks.

## Task
Analyze the FULL TEXT of an educational document and identify ALL structural chapter/section headings.
Each line is a text block: [ID|pageNumber|optionalTypeTag] text

## CRITICAL: Table of Contents vs. Document Body
The first few pages may contain a TABLE OF CONTENTS (listing chapter names with page numbers).
You MUST IGNORE the actual Table of Contents lists.
HOWEVER, do NOT ignore "Front Matter" sections (like Introduction, Preface) or "Unit" title pages that appear before the main chapters.

Table of contents signs (IGNORE these):
- Pages explicitly labeled "Contents" or "Table of Contents"
- Lists of titles followed by page numbers (e.g., "1.1 ... 5", "Chapter 2 ... 12")
- Dense lists of headings on a single page

## What ARE Structural Headings (INCLUDE)
- **Front Matter**: Introduction, Preface, How to use this book, Acknowledgements, etc.
- **Back Matter**: Glossary, Index, Answers, etc.
- **Organization Containers**: Units, Modules, Parts, Themes (e.g., "Unit 1", "Module A")
- **Chapters**: (e.g., "Chapter 1", "1 Review of number concepts")
- **Sections**: (e.g., "1.1 Different types of numbers")
- **Sub-sections**: (e.g., "1.1.1", "Case Study")
- **Assessments**: "Past paper questions", "Project", "Review", "Summary"

## What Are NOT Headings (MUST EXCLUDE)
- The actual Table of Contents list itself
- Instructional labels: "Key points", "Tip", "Note", "Activity", "Exercise", "Q&A"
- Problem type labels: "Question 1", "Section A", "Multiple Choice"
- Running headers/footers (often repeated at top/bottom of pages)

## Hierarchy (Map to logical levels)
        - **Level 1**: Top-level containers.
          - Front Matter / Back Matter categories (e.g., "Front Matter", "Back Matter")
          - Units / Parts / Modules / Themes (e.g., "Unit 1", "Part A")
          - Standalone Chapters if no Units exist
        - **Level 2**: Main content divisions.
          - Chapters (if inside a Unit)
          - Sections (if inside a standalone Chapter)
          - Specific Front/Back Matter sections (e.g., "Introduction", "Foreword", "Glossary")
        - **Level 3**: Sub-divisions.
          - Sections (if inside a Unit->Chapter)
          - Sub-sections
          - Specific items like "1.1 Understanding units"
        - **Level 4**: Deep sub-divisions (if needed).
          - Sub-sections (e.g., "1.1.1")

        ## Fragmented Titles
If a heading is split across consecutive blocks (e.g., block 100="Chapter" + block 101="1"), merge them: output id as [100, 101].

## Completeness
- Include EVERY structural heading from the very beginning (Introduction) to the very end (Index).
- Do not skip "Unit" pages.

## Output Format
Return ONLY valid JSON:
{
  "directory": [
    {"id": <number or [n1, n2]>, "level": <1|2|3>, "title": "<heading text>"},
    ...
  ]
}
Array MUST be in the LOGICAL order of the document.

## CRITICAL RULE: RETURN ORIGINAL IDs
The block IDs you see (e.g., [12479|p394|T1]) are GLOBAL IDs. You MUST return these exact IDs. DO NOT generate your own sequential IDs starting from 0.
- CORRECT: {"id": 12479, ...}
- WRONG: {"id": 0, ...} (when the block shown was [12479|...])

[12484|p395|T1] Interquartile range
[12485|p395] As with the range, the interquartile range gives a measure of how spread out or consistent the data is. The main difference is that the interquartile range (IQR) avoids using extreme data by finding t...
[12486|p395] [Equation]
[12487|p395] If one set of data has a smaller IQR than another set, then the first set is more consistent and less spread out. This can be a useful comparison tool.

[12625|p400|T1] SUMMARY
[12626|p400|T1] Do you know ...?
[12627|p400] Averages - the mode, median and mean - are used to summarise a collection of data.
[12628|p400] Discrete data can be listed or arranged in a frequency distribution.
[12629|p400] Continuous data can be listed or arranged into groups.
[12630|p400] The mean is affected by extreme data.
[12631|p400] The median is less affected by extreme data.
[12632|p400] The lower quartile [EQ] lies [EQ] of the way through the data.
[12633|p400] The upper quartile [EQ] lies [EQ] of the way through the data.
[12634|p400] The interquartile range [EQ] gives a measure of how spread out or consistent the central [EQ] of the data is.
[12635|p400] The range is the difference between the largest and smallest values and also measures spread, but it is affected by extreme values and is used less often than the interquartile range.

[12837|p410|T1] Unit 3 Project
[12838|p410|T1] Litov's mean value theorem
[12839|p410] Start with any two numbers, for example 8 and 2.
[12840|p410] Generate a sequence where the next number is the mean of the previous two numbers.
[12841|p410] So the next number is half of [EQ] , and the sequence continues: 8, 2, 5.
[12842|p410] The next number is half of [EQ] , and the sequence continues: 8, 2, 5, 3.5.
[12843|p410] What would happen if you continued this process indefinitely?
[12844|p410] Choose a few pairs of starting numbers and repeat the process.
[12845|p410] Each time, your sequence should get closer and closer to a value which we call the limit.
[12846|p410] Can you find a relationship between your starting numbers and the limit of the sequence they generate?
[12847|p410] Can you explain why this happens?
[12848|p410|T1] Investigate starting with three numbers
[12849|p410] Generate a sequence where the next number is the mean of the previous three numbers.
[12850|p410] - Check you agree that if we start with 4, 1, 10, the next number is 5, and the number after that is [EQ] .
[12851|p410] What would happen if you continued this process indefinitely?
[12852|p410] Choose some more sets of three starting numbers.
[12853|p410] Can you find a relationship between your starting numbers and the limit of the sequence they generate?
[12854|p410] Can you explain why this happens?
[12855|p410|T1] GO FURTHER
[12856|p410] Explore what happens when you have [EQ] starting numbers and you generate a sequence where the next number is the mean of the last [EQ] numbers.

[12996|p416|T1] 13.2 Time
[12997|p416] You have already learnt how to tell the time and you should know how to read and write time using the 12-hour and 24-hour systems.
[12998|p416|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 2
[12999|p416] Sara left home at 2.15 p.m. and returned at 3.50 p.m. How long was Sara away from home?
[13000|p416|T1] Answer
[13001|p416] From 2.15p.m. to 3.00p.m. is 45 minutes Start by counting on to the next From 3.00p.m. to 3.50p.m. is 50 minutes whole hour.
[13002|p416] 45 + 50 = 95 minutes Add together the times in minutes.
[13003|p416] = 1 hour and 35 minutes Convert to hours and minutes.
[13004|p416|T1] INVESTIGATION
[13005|p416|T1] Babylonian mathematics

[13008|p416] 2 Our decimal system is useful because we have 10 fingers and thumbs on our hands, divided into 2 lots of 5. Look at the factors of 60 and carry out some research to try to find out why the Babylonian...
[13009|p416] 3 The Babylonians also seem to be the reason why we have 360 degrees in a full circle. What unit of time do you know that is divided into just over 360 units? How does this relate to a full turn of a ...
[13010|p416] The next example shows you how to calculate the duration of an event.

[13028|p417|T1] TIP
[13029|p417] Calculators work differently, so learn how the time functions work on your calculator. Try to use your calculator to get the same answers as in the worked examples. You can also use this to check your...
[13030|p417] The methods you have seen in the examples so far are best used when you are dealing with time within the same day. But what happens when the time difference goes from one day to another day?
[13031|p417|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 4
[13032|p417] How much time passes from 19:35 on Monday to 03:55 on Tuesday?
[13033|p417|T1] Answer
[13034|p417] 19:35 to 24:00 is one part and 00:00 to 03:55 the next day is the other part.
[13035|p417] A good way to start this problem is to divide the time into parts.
[13036|p417|T1] Part one:
[13037|p417] 19:35 to 20:00 is 25 minutes 20:00 to 24:00 is 4 hours So, 19:35 to 24:00 is 4 h 25 min
[13038|p417] Work out how much time passes to reach midnight.
[13039|p417|T1] Part two:
[13040|p417] 0:00 to 03:55 is 3 h 55 min
[13041|p417] 03:55 is 3 hours and 55 minutes past midnight.
[13042|p417] 4h 25min + 3h 55min = 7h 80min = 8h 20min
[13043|p417] Add the result of the two parts together.
[13044|p417] The total time is 8 hours and 20 minutes.
[13045|p417] 80 minutes = 1 hour 20 minutes

[13126|p420|T1] 13.3 Limits of accuracy - upper and lower bounds
[13127|p420] Measurements are always given to a certain level of accuracy. The limits of accuracy of any measurement depends on the instruments used to measure it, but even with very accurate scientific measuring ...
[13128|p420] [Image]
[13129|p421|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[13130|p421] You learnt to round numbers in Chapter 1. Make sure that you remember how rounding works before continuing with this section.
[13131|p421] The way we round numbers means that all measurements have to be between certain limits. These limits depend on the level of accuracy that was used to round the measurement.
[13132|p421] The smallest value a measurement can take is called the lower bound. The greatest value it can take is called the upper bound. The difference between the upper and lower bounds is called the error int...
[13133|p421] Read through the next example to see how these bounds apply in real life.
[13134|p421] Raeman operates a crane at a building site. The manufacturer states that the crane can safely lift a maximum of 6.2 tonnes. Raeman needs to move some 1.2 tonne blocks, where the mass of each block is ...
[13135|p421] But, the mass of the blocks has been rounded to 1 decimal place. What if the blocks all weigh 1.245 tonnes? They all round to 1.2 tonnes to 1 decimal place, but the total mass of 5 blocks is now:
[13136|p421] [EQ] tonnes.
[13137|p421] This is more than the crane can lift safely.
[13138|p421] How can Raeman work out the maximum number of blocks he can lift safely?
[13139|p421] [Image]
[13140|p421|T1] LINK
[13141|p421] The Hubble Space Telescope, launched into space in 1990, was originally made with a [EQ] error in the shape of the lens. This caused many of the images to be blurred and a repair had to be made in 199...
[13142|p421] [Image]
[13143|p421|T1] Finding the greatest and least possible values of a rounded measurement
[13144|p421] In the crane example 1.2 tonnes is rounded to 1 decimal place. It can be useful to work out the greatest and least possible values of the actual measurement.
[13145|p421] If you place 1.2 on a number line, you can see much more clearly what the range of possible values are:
[13146|p421] [Image]
[13147|p421] At the upper end, the range of possible values stops at 1.25. If you round 1.25 to 1 decimal place you get 1.3. Although 1.25 does not round to 1.2 (to 1 decimal place).
[13148|p422] it is still used as the upper value. However, you should understand that the actual value can be anything up to but not including 1.25. The largest possible value is called the upper bound. Similarly,...
[13149|p422|T1] TIP
[13150|p422] To find the limits think about what 1 decimal place means. One decimal place is 0.1. If you divide 0.1 by 2 you get 0.05. Add 0.05 to find the upper limit and subtract 0.05 to find the lower limit. Wr...
[13151|p422] [Equation]
[13152|p422] If [EQ] represents the mass (in tonnes) of the block, then you can express the error interval as:
[13153|p422] [Equation]
[13154|p422] This is called inequality notation and it shows that the true value of [EQ] lies between 1.15 (including 1.15) and 1.25 (not including 1.25).
[13155|p422|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 5
[13156|p422] Find the upper and lower bounds of the following, taking into account the level of rounding shown in each case.
[13157|p422] a [EQ] to the nearest cm
[13158|p422] b 22.5, to 1 decimal place
[13159|p422] c 128000,to 3 significant figures
[13160|p422] d 120, to the nearest 20
[13161|p422|T1] Answers
[13162|p422] a Show [EQ] on a number line. The real value will be closest to [EQ] if it lies between the lower bound of [EQ] and the upper bound of [EQ] .
[13163|p422] b Look at 22.5 on a number line. The real value will be closest to 22.5 if it lies between the lower bound of 22.45 and the upper bound of 22.55.
[13164|p422] 128000 is shown on a number line. 128000 lies between the lower bound of 127500 and the upper bound of 128500.
[13165|p422] 120 is shown on a number line. 120 lies between the lower bound of 110 and the upper bound of 130.

[13203|p423|T1] Problem-solving with upper and lower bounds
[13204|p423] Some calculations use more than one rounded value. You can use the upper and lower bounds of each value to find the upper and lower bounds for the calculated answer, but you need to work carefully.
[13205|p423|T1] TIP
[13206|p423] If you get confused when dealing with upper and lower bounds, draw a number line to help you.
[13207|p424|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 6
[13208|p424] The dimensions of a rectangular rug are given as [EQ] correct to the nearest metre.
[13209|p424] Find the greatest and smallest values for the area of this rug.
[13210|p424|T1] Answer
[13211|p424] [EQ]
[13212|p424] Find the upper and lower bounds of each measurement.
[13213|p424] [EQ]
[13214|p424] Smallest possible area:
[13215|p424] [EQ]
[13216|p424] Multiply the lower bounds of the measurements to find the smallest possible area.
[13217|p424] Greatest possible area:
[13218|p424] [EQ]
[13219|p424] Multiply the upper bounds of the measurements to find the greatest possible area.
[13220|p424|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 7
[13221|p424] An antelope runs across a riverbed that is [EQ] wide (to the nearest 10 metres) at a speed of 3.9 metres per second (to 1 decimal place). What is the least and greatest possible time it will take to r...
[13222|p424|T1] Answer
[13223|p424] Start by finding the upper and lower bounds of each measurement.
[13224|p424] Draw a two-way table to relate the measurements.
[13225|p424] Divide the distance in each column by each speed.
[13226|p424] [Table Row] <tr><td>Speed\Distance</td><td>375</td><td>385</td></tr>
[13227|p424] [Table Row] <tr><td>3.85</td><td>97.40</td><td>100</td></tr>
[13228|p424] [Table Row] <tr><td>3.95</td><td>94.94</td><td>97.47</td></tr>
[13229|p424] [Table Row] <tr><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr>
[13230|p424] The greatest possible time is 100 seconds. The least possible time is 94.94 seconds (correct to 2 decimal places).

[13329|p428] Measurements are not ever exact.
[13330|p428] Any measurement will fall between limits called the upper and lower bound.
[13331|p428] You can write a measurement as an inequality that shows the error interval (what the measurement could be).
[13332|p428] When you use approximate measurements in calculations you combine small errors to get a bigger error and the answer will be less accurate than if you only round the final answer.

[13338|p429|T1] 13.4 Conversion graphs
[13339|p429] So far in this chapter, you have seen that it is possible to convert between different units in the metric system. Another measuring system is the imperial system. Sometimes you might need to convert ...
[13340|p429] You can use conversion graphs when you need to convert from one measurement to another, for example from miles (imperial) to kilometres (metric) or from dollars to yen.
[13341|p429] Eight kilometres is approximately equal to five miles.
[13342|p429] If you travel no distance in kilometres then you also travel no distance in miles.
[13343|p429] These two facts mean you can draw a graph for converting between miles and kilometres.
[13344|p429] If you extend the line far enough you can read higher values. Notice, for example, that the line passes through the point with coordinates (25, 40). This means that 25 miles is approximately 40 km.

[13402|p432|T1] 13.5 Exchanging currencies

[13404|p432] Calculating money involves working with decimals, because most currencies are decimals. Remember though, when you calculate with money you need to include the units (\$ or cents) in your answers.
[13405|p432] The money a country uses is called its currency. Each country has its own currency and most currencies work on a decimal system (100 small units are equal to one main unit). The following table shows ...

[13449|p434|T1] SUMMARY
[13450|p434|T1] Do you know ...?
[13451|p434] There are several measuring systems; the most common are metric and imperial.
[13452|p434] Time is measured in different units, so you have to consider the conversion factors when you work with time.
[13453|p434] Every measurement quoted to a given accuracy has a lower bound and an upper bound. The actual value of a measurement is greater than or equal to the lower bound, but strictly less than the upper bound...
[13454|p434] You can draw a graph to help convert between different systems of units.
[13455|p434] Countries use different currencies and you can convert between them if you know the exchange rate.

[14047|p454] This is satisfied by any value of [EQ] that is greater than -3, for example -2, -1, 2.4, 3.5, 10 ...
[14048|p454] If you calculate the value of [EQ] for each of these values you get 13, 8, -9, -14.5, -47 ... and not one of these works in the original inequality as they are all smaller than 18.
[14049|p454] Here is an alternative solution:
[14050|p454] [Equation]
[14051|p454] [Equation]
[14052|p454] [Equation]
[14053|p454] This is a correct solution, and the final answer is very similar to the 'wrong' one above.
[14054|p454] The only difference is that the inequality symbol has been reversed.
[14055|p455] Why does this happen? When you multiply both sides by a negative value, the side that was greater will end up being a 'bigger' negative number, which actually means it is now less than the other side!
[14056|p455] You need to remember the following:
[14057|p455] If you multiply or divide both sides of an inequality by a negative number then you must reverse the direction of the inequality.
[14058|p455] If you can avoid negatives, by adding or subtracting terms, then try to do so.
[14059|p455|T1] REFLECTION
[14060|p455] Have you completely understood this warning? How will you make sure you remember to reverse the inequality symbol at the right moment each time?

[14550|p472|T1] 14.7 Algebraic fractions
[14551|p472] You will now use several of the techniques covered so far in this chapter to simplify complex algebraic fractions.
[14552|p472] You already know that you can simplify fractions by dividing the numerator and denominator by a common factor. This can also be done with algebraic fractions.

[14704|p478|T1] SUMMARY
[14705|p478|T1] Do you know ...?
[14706|p478] Simultaneous means at the same time.
[14707|p478] The intersection of two straight lines is the simultaneous solution of their equations.
[14708|p478] Simultaneous linear equations can be solved graphically or algebraically.
[14709|p478] Inequalities represent a range of solutions.
[14710|p478] Inequalities in one variable can be represented on a number line and inequalities in two variables as a region on a plane.
[14711|p478] A quadratic expression, [EQ] can be written in the completed square form as [EQ] .
[14712|p478] Quadratic equations that do not factorise can be solved by completing the square or by using the quadratic formula.
[14713|p478] [Equation]
[14714|p478] Complex algebraic fractions can be simplified by factorising and cancelling like terms.
[14715|p478|T1] Are you able to ...?
[14716|p478] solve simultaneous linear equations graphically
[14717|p478] solve simultaneous linear equations algebraically
[14718|p478] show an inequality in one variable on a number line.
[14719|p478] show an inequality in two variables as a region in the [EQ] plane.
[14720|p478] show a region in the [EQ] plane that satisfies more than one inequality.
[14721|p478] 1. rewrite a quadratic expression in completed square form.
[14722|p478] To solve a quadratic equation using the completed square or the quadratic formula,
[14723|p478] simplify complex algebraic fractions.

[15156|p499|T1] Sine and cosine ratios
[15157|p499] You will have noticed that the tangent ratio only makes use of the opposite and adjacent sides. What happens if you need to use the hypotenuse? In fact, there are three possible pairs of sides that yo...
[15158|p499] - opposite and adjacent (already used with the tangent ratio)
[15159|p499] - opposite and hypotenuse
[15160|p499] or adjacent and hypotenuse.
[15161|p500] This means that you need two more ratios:
[15162|p500] The sine ratio is written as [EQ]
[15163|p500] adj(A) the cosine ratio is written as cos(A) = hyp
[15164|p500] As with the tangent ratio, you can use the sin and cos keys on your calculator to find the sine and cosine ratios associated with given angles. You can also use the shift or sin and shift or cos 'inve...
[15165|p500] Before looking at some worked examples you should note that with three possible ratios you need to know how to pick the right one! 'SOHCAHTOA' might help you to remember:
[15166|p500] [Image]
[15167|p500] The letters 'SOHCAHTOA' can be divided into three triangles of letters, each representing one of the three trigonometric ratios. The first letter in each trio tells you which ratio it represents (sine...
[15168|p500] For example, if a problem involves the opposite and hypotenuse, you simply need to find the triangle of letters that includes 'O' and 'H': SOH.
[15169|p500] The 'S' tells you that it is the sine ratio, the 'O' at the top of the triangle sits on top of the fraction, and the lower 'H' sits on the bottom.
[15170|p500] The use of 'SOHCAHTOA' is shown clearly in the following worked examples. The tangent ratio has been included again in these examples to help show you how to decide which ratio should be used.
[15171|p500|T1] TIP
[15172|p500] S = sine
[15173|p500] [EQ] opposite
[15174|p500] H = hypotenuse
[15175|p500] C = cosine
[15176|p500] A = adjacent
[15177|p500] T = tan
[15178|p500] [Image]

[15427|p510|T1] 15.5 Solving problems using trigonometry
[15428|p510] You may need to use more than one trigonometric ratio when you solve problems that involve right-angled triangles. To make it clear which ratio to use and when, you should follow these guidelines.
[15429|p510] - If the question does not include a diagram, draw one. Make it clear and large.
[15430|p510] - Identify any right-angled triangles that may be useful to you.
[15431|p510] - Draw each triangle separately and clearly label any sides and angles that you already know.
[15432|p510] - Write down which of the opposite, adjacent and hypotenuse you are going to use, and then use SOHCAHTOA to help you decide which ratio to use.
[15433|p510] Write down the ratio and solve, either for an angle or a side.
[15434|p510] - If you need to use a side or angle that you have calculated for another part of a question, try to use the unrounded value that you have in your calculator memory. This will help to avoid rounding e...

[15531|p516|T1] 15.6 Sines, cosines and tangents of angles greater than [EQ]

[15671|p521|T1] The sine rule

[15674|p521] These relationships are usually expressed in one go:
[15675|p521] [Equation]
[15676|p521] This is the sine rule. This version of the rule, with the sine ratios placed on the tops of the fractions, is normally used to calculate angles.
[15677|p521] The formulae can also be turned upside down when you want to calculate lengths:
[15678|p521] [Equation]
[15679|p521] You should remember that this represents three possible relationships.
[15680|p521] Notice that in each case, both the upper and lower case form of each letter is used. This means that each fraction that you use requires an angle and the length of its opposite side.
[15681|p521|T1] TIP
[15682|p521] Remember, the sine rule is used when you are dealing with pairs of opposite sides and angles.

[15697|p522|T1] The ambiguous case of the sine rule

[15699|p522] Worked example 18 demonstrates how this may happen.

[16091|p542] - recognise the common errors that are often made with scatter diagrams.

[16139|p546|T1] Drawing a line of best fit
[16140|p546] You can also show the general trend by drawing a line of best fit. In the diagram a line has been drawn so that it passes as close to as many points as possible.
[16141|p546] [Image]
[16142|p546] This is the line of best fit and you can use it to make predictions based on the collected data.
[16143|p546] For example, if you want to try to predict the ice cream sales on a day with a temperature of [EQ] , you carry out the following steps:
[16144|p546] 1. Locate [EQ] on the temperature axis.
[16145|p546] 2 Draw a clear line vertically from this point to the line of best fit.
[16146|p546] 3 Draw a horizontal line from the point on the line of best fit to the sales axis.
[16147|p546] 4 Read the sales value from the graph.
[16148|p546] The diagram now looks like this and the estimated value for the sales is approximately $575.

[16174|p548|T1] Extrapolation
[16175|p548] Before you draw and interpret some scatter diagrams for yourself you should be aware that there may be problems with making predictions outside the range of the collected data.
[16176|p548] In Worked example 1, the data does not include any heights above [EQ] . The trend may not continue or it may look different for greater heights. So, for example, you should not try to predict the foot...
[16177|p548] The process of extending the line of best fit beyond the collected data is called extrapolation.

[16225|p551] [Image]
[16226|p552|T1] SUMMARY
[16227|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>Do you know ...?</td></tr>
[16228|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>You can use a scatter diagram to investigate the strength of any relationship between two variables.</td></tr>
[16229|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>If one of the variables generally increases as the other variable increases, then you say that there is a positive correlation.</td></tr>
[16230|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>If one of the variables generally decreases as the other variable increases, then you say that there is a negative correlation.</td></tr>
[16231|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>The clearer the relationship, the stronger the correlation.</td></tr>
[16232|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>You can draw a line of best fit if the points seem to lie close to a straight line.</td></tr>
[16233|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>You can use the line of best fit to predict values of one variable from values of the other.</td></tr>
[16234|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>You should only make predictions using a line of best fit that has been drawn within the range of the data.</td></tr>
[16235|p552] [Table Row] <tr><td>Are you able to ...?</td></tr>

[16406|p561|T1] Marbles in a box
[16407|p561] Consider an imaginary three-dimensional game where two players take it in turn to place different coloured marbles into a box.
[16408|p561] The box is made from 27 transparent unit cubes arranged in a 3-by-3-by-3 array.
[16409|p561] [Image]
[16410|p561] The aim of the game is to make a winning row of three of your own marbles. Rows can be horizontal, vertical or diagonal.
[16411|p561] This diagram shows the placement of one winning row. The marbles are on three levels and the row is diagonal.
[16412|p561] [Image]
[16413|p561] How many different ways are there to make a winning row?
[16414|p561] Explain clearly how you got to your answer.
[16415|p561|T1] TIP
[16416|p561] Take note that in this diagram the edges of the cubes are not shown, just the placement of the marbles on each level.

[16462|p564|T1] 17.1 Earning money
[16463|p564] When people are employed they are paid for their work. Earnings can be worked out in different ways:
[16464|p564] - Wages - pay based on a fixed number of hours worked, usually paid weekly. Extra hours of work are called overtime and these are usually paid at a higher rate.
[16465|p564] Salary - pay based on a fixed yearly amount, usually paid monthly. Overtime may be paid, or workers may be given time off in exchange for extra time spent at work.
[16466|p564] - Piece work - pay based on the number of items produced.
[16467|p564] - Commission - pay is based on a percentage of sales made; sometimes a low wage, called a retainer, is paid as well as commission.
[16468|p564|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 1
[16469|p564] Emmanuel makes beaded necklaces. He is paid in South African rand at a rate of R24.50 per completed necklace. He is able to make 55 necklaces per week. Calculate his weekly earnings from the sale of n...
[16470|p564|T1] Answer
[16471|p564] Earnings [EQ] Multiply items produced by the rate paid.
[16472|p564] [EQ]
[16473|p564|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 2
[16474|p564] Sanjay works as a sales representative for a company that sells mobile phones in the United Arab Emirates. He is paid a retainer of 800 Dhs (dirhams) per week plus a commission of [EQ] of all sales.
[16475|p564] a How much would he earn in a week if he made no sales?
[16476|p564] b How much would he earn if he sold four phones at 3299 Dhs each in a week?
[16477|p564|T1] Answers
[16478|p564] a 800 Dhs
[16479|p564] If he made no sales, he would earn no commission, only his retainer.
[16480|p564] b Commission [EQ] of [EQ]
[16481|p564] Calculate [EQ] of the total sales Sanjay made.
[16482|p564] [EQ]
[16483|p564] [EQ] Dhs
[16484|p564] Earnings [EQ] retainer [EQ] commission
[16485|p564] [EQ]
[16486|p564] [EQ]
[16487|p564] Add this to the retainer of 800 Dhs.
[16488|p564|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[16489|p564] You learnt about the decimal equivalents of percentages in Chapter 5.
[16490|p565|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 3
[16491|p565] Josh's hourly rate of pay is \(12.50. He is paid 'time-and-a-half' for work after hours and on Saturdays and 'double-time' for Sundays and Public Holidays.
[16492|p565] One week he worked 5.5 hours on Saturday and 3 hours on Sunday. How much overtime pay would he earn?
[16493|p565|T1] Answer
[16494|p565] Saturday overtime = 1.5 × [EQ]103.13 time)
[16495|p565] Saturday overtime [EQ] 12.50 \times 3\) (double-time [EQ] normal time) [EQ]
[16496|p565] Total overtime = [EQ]75.00 = $178.13

[16502|p565] a $67.50 for five hours
[16503|p565] b $245.10 for a 38-hour week
[16504|p565] c $126.23 for 13.5 hours

[16508|p565] 5 A team of workers in a factory is paid $23.25 per pallet of goods produced. If a team of five workers produces 102 pallets in a shift, how much will each person in the team have earned that shift?
[16509|p565] 6 An estate agent is paid a retainer of [EQ]150 000 and 1.75% on amounts above that. How much would she earn in a week if she sold a house for [EQ]125 000?
[16510|p565|T1] TIP
[16511|p565] Commissions like these are on a sliding scale. So if the agent had sales of [EQ] 150000 and 1.75% of the $10000 that is above this threshold.

[16520|p566|T1] Deductions from earnings
[16521|p566] Gross income (earnings) is the total amount a person earns.
[16522|p566] Deductions, such as income tax, pension contributions, unemployment and health insurance, and union dues are often taken from the gross earnings before the person is paid. The amount that is left over...
[16523|p566] Net income [EQ] gross income - deductions

[16534|p566|T1] TIP
[16535|p566] Gross earnings, deductions and net income are normally shown on a payment advice (slip) which is given to each worker when they get paid.
[16536|p566|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS

[16611|p569|T1] 17.2 Borrowing and investing money
[16612|p569] When you borrow money or you buy things on credit, you are normally charged interest for the use of the money. Similarly, when you save or invest money, you are paid interest by the bank or financial ...
[16613|p570|T1] Simple interest
[16614|p570] Simple interest is a fixed percentage of the original amount borrowed or invested. In other words, if you borrow [EQ] per year, you will be charged \)5 interest for every year of the loan.
[16615|p570] Simple interest involves adding the interest amount to the original amount at regular intervals. The formula used to calculate simple interest is:
[16616|p570] [Equation]
[16617|p570] [EQ] the principal, which is the original amount borrowed or saved

[16619|p570] [Equation]

[16638|p571] Interest = amount - principal

[16642|p571] Total interest [EQ] annual interest [EQ]
[16643|p571] So it will take three years for [EQ] 310 at the rate of 8% per annum simple interest.

[16696|p573|T1] Hire purchase
[16697|p573] Many people buy items such as TVs, furniture and cars on a system of payment called hire purchase (HP).
[16698|p573] On HP you pay a part of the price as a deposit and the remainder in a certain number of weekly or monthly instalments. Interest is charged on outstanding balances. It is useful to be able to work out ...
[16699|p573|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 9
[16700|p573] The cash price of a car is \(20000. The hire purchase price is \)6000 deposit and instalments of \(700 per month for two years. How much more than the cash price is the hire purchase price?
[16701|p573|T1] Answer
[16702|p573] [Equation]
[16703|p573] [Equation]
[16704|p573] 24 instalments = [EQ]16800 (once per month over two years is 24 monthly instalments)
[16705|p573] Total HP price [EQ] deposit [EQ] 24 instalments
[16706|p573] [Equation]
[16707|p573] The hire purchase price is $2800 more than the cash price.
[16708|p573|T1] TIP
[16709|p573] In HP agreements, the deposit is sometimes called the down-payment.
[16710|p573] When interest is calculated as a proportion of the amount owed it is called a flat rate of interest. This is the same as simple interest.
[16711|p574|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 10
[16712|p574] A person bought a car for $30000 on hire purchase. The deposit was 20% and interest on the outstanding balance for the period of repayment is at 10% per annum. If the balance is to be paid in 12 equal...
[16713|p574|T1] Answer
[16714|p574] Cash price = $30000
[16715|p574] Deposit of [EQ] 6000$
[16716|p574] Outstanding balance = [EQ]6000 = $24000
[16717|p574] Interest of [EQ] 2400$
[16718|p574] Amount to be paid by instalments = outstanding balance + interest
[16719|p574] [Equation]
[16720|p574] Each instalment = 26400/12 = $2200 (divide by total number of instalments)

[16904|p582|T1] 17.3 Buying and selling
[16905|p582] When people trade, they buy goods, mark them up (decide on a price) and then sell them.
[16906|p582] The price the trader pays for goods is called the cost price.
[16907|p582] The price the goods are sold at is called the selling price.
[16908|p582] If the selling price is higher than the cost price, the goods are sold at a profit.
[16909|p582] If the selling price is lower than the cost price, the goods are sold at a loss.
[16910|p582] Profit = selling price - cost price
[16911|p582] Loss [EQ] cost price - selling price
[16912|p583|T1] Calculating the selling price, cost price and mark up
[16913|p583] People who sell goods have to decide how much profit they want to make. In other words, they have to decide by how much they will mark up the cost price to make the selling price.
[16914|p583] Cost price + % mark up = selling price
[16915|p583] The cost price is always [EQ] . If you add [EQ] mark up, the selling price will be [EQ] .
[16916|p583|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 18
[16917|p583] At a market, a trader makes a profit of [EQ] 6.48.
[16918|p583] What is his percentage profit?
[16919|p583|T1] Answer
[16920|p583] Cost price = [EQ]1.08 = $5.40
[16921|p583] Subtract the profit from the selling price to find the cost price of the item.
[16922|p583] profit Percentage profit [EQ] cost price [EQ]
[16923|p583] [Equation]

[16931|p584|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 20

[16936|p584] So, \( \\( {39} = {130}\% \times \) selling price
[16937|p584] To find [EQ]
[16938|p584] [Equation]
[16939|p584] The cost price is $30.
[16940|p584] Divide by 130 to find [EQ] and then multiply by 100 to find [EQ] (which is the cost price).

[16957|p585|T1] Discount
[16958|p585] If items are not being sold as quickly as a shop would like or if they want to clear a stock as new fashions come out, then goods may be sold at a discount
[16959|p585] Discount can be treated in the same way as percentage change (loss) as long as you remember that the percentage change is always calculated as a percentage of the original amount.

[17005|p586] [Table Row] <tr><td>The formula for calculating compound interest is V = P(1 + r/100)^n.</td></tr>
[17006|p586] [Table Row] <tr><td>Hire purchase (HP) is a method of buying goods on credit and paying for them in instalments which include a flat rate of interest added to the original price.</td></tr>
[17007|p586] [Table Row] <tr><td>When goods are sold at a profit they are sold for more than they cost. When they are sold at a loss they are sold for less than they cost. The original price is called the cost pri...
[17008|p586] [Table Row] <tr><td>A discount is a reduction in the usual price of an item. A discount of 15% means you pay 15% less than the usual price.</td></tr>

[17113|p593|T1] 18.1 Review of quadratic graphs (the parabola)
[17114|p593|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[17115|p593] You have learnt how to use a table of values to draw curved graphs with equations of the form [EQ] in earlier stages of your studies. You are going to revise that work and then learn how to plot quadr...
[17116|p593] In Chapter 10 you learnt that quadratic equations have an [EQ] term as their highest power. The simplest quadratic equation for a quadratic graph is [EQ] .
[17117|p593] Here is a table showing the values for [EQ] from [EQ] .
[17118|p593] [Table Row] <tr><td>x</td><td>-3</td><td>-2</td><td>-1</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td></tr>
[17119|p593] [Table Row] <tr><td>y=x2</td><td>9</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>9</td></tr>
[17120|p593] You can use these points to plot and draw a graph just as you did with linear equations. The graph of a quadratic relationship is called a parabola
[17121|p593] Here is the table of values for [EQ] from [EQ] .
[17122|p593] [Table Row] <tr><td>x</td><td>-3</td><td>-2</td><td>-1</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td></tr>
[17123|p593] [Table Row] <tr><td>y=-x2</td><td>-9</td><td>-4</td><td>-1</td><td>0</td><td>-1</td><td>-4</td><td>-9</td></tr>
[17124|p593] When you plot these points and draw the parabola you can see that the negative sign in front of the [EQ] has the effect of turning the graph so that it faces downwards.

[17834|p628|T1] 18.6 Finding the gradient of a curve
[17835|p628] This simple graph of height against distance shows the route followed by a mountain biker on a trail.
[17836|p628] [Image]
[17837|p628] Some parts of the trail have a steep positive gradient, some have a gradual positive gradient, some parts are level and other parts have a negative gradient. It should be clear from this graph, that a...
[17838|p628] You cannot find the gradient of a whole curve but you can find the gradient of a point on the curve by drawing a tangent to it.
[17839|p628] The gradient of a curve at a point is the gradient of the tangent to the curve at that point. Once you have drawn the tangent to a curve, you can work out the gradient of
[17840|p628] the tangent just as you would for a straight line (gradient [EQ] ).
[17841|p629] Look at the graph to see how this works. [EQ] is the tangent to the curve at point [EQ]
[17842|p629] [Image]
[17843|p629|T1] How to draw the tangent
[17844|p629] [Image]
[17845|p629] Mark a point on the curve [EQ] .
[17846|p629] [Image]
[17847|p629] Place your ruler against the curve so that it touches it only at point [EQ]
[17848|p629] [Image]
[17849|p629] Use a pencil to draw the tangent.
[17850|p629|T1] TIP
[17851|p629] If you extend your tangent, it may touch the curve again at a completely different point, but this is not a problem.
[17852|p629] If the tangent is rising from left to right, its gradient is positive. If the tangent is falling from left to right, its gradient is negative.
[17853|p629] Upwards = positive
[17854|p629] Downwards = negative
[17855|p629|T1] Calculating the gradient of a tangent
[17856|p629] Mark two points, [EQ] and [EQ] , on the tangent. Try to make the horizontal distance between [EQ] and [EQ] a whole number of units (measured on the [EQ] -axis scale).
[17857|p629] Draw a horizontal line through [EQ] and a vertical line through [EQ] to form a right-angled triangle [EQ] .
[17858|p629] [Image]
[17859|p629] Gradient of the curve at [EQ] gradient of the tangent [EQ]
[17860|p629] distance [EQ] (measured on the [EQ] -axis scale) distance [EQ] (measured on the [EQ] -axis scale)
[17861|p629|T1] TIP
[17862|p629] You must measure NQ and PN according to the scales on the y-axis and x-axis respectively. One of the most common mistakes is not doing this!

[18223|p645|T1] Do you know ...?
[18224|p645] The graph of a quadratic equation is a recognisable curve called a parabola.
[18225|p645] A reciprocal equation is an equation of the form [EQ] or [EQ] .
[18226|p645] The graph of a reciprocal equation is a two-part curve called a hyperbola.
[18227|p645] You can use graphs to solve equations by finding the value of [EQ] or [EQ] at different points on the graph.
[18228|p645] You can find the solution to simultaneous equations using the points of intersection of two graphs.
[18229|p645] Linear, quadratic, cubic and reciprocal terms can occur in the same equation. It is possible to draw graphs of these curves by constructing a table of values and then plotting the points.
[18230|p645] An exponential equation has the form [EQ] . These equations produce steep curved graphs.
[18231|p645] You can draw a tangent to a curve and use it to find the gradient of the curve at the point where the tangent touches it.
[18232|p645] You can differentiate functions to find gradients and stationary (turning) points.

[18372|p651|T1] 19.1 Symmetry in two dimensions
[18373|p651] There are two types of symmetry in two-dimensional shapes:
[18374|p651] .
[18375|p651] .
[18376|p651|T1] Line symmetry
[18377|p651] If a shape can be folded so that one half fits exactly over the other half, it has line symmetry (also called reflection symmetry).
[18378|p651] Triangle A is symmetrical. The dotted line divides it into two identical parts.
[18379|p651] [Image]
[18380|p651] Triangle B is not symmetrical. You cannot draw a line which will divide it into two identical halves.
[18381|p651] [Image]
[18382|p651] If you place a mirror on the dividing line on shape A, the view in the mirror will be that of the whole triangle. The line is called the line of symmetry or mirror line of the shape.
[18383|p651|T1] LINK
[18384|p651] Molecular symmetry is an important concept in chemistry. When a molecule has symmetry, it means that certain parts of it can be interchanged with other parts without changing the identity of the molec...
[18385|p651] For example, if you rotate a water molecule by [EQ] about an axis passing through the central O atom (between the two H atoms) it will look the same as before.
[18386|p651] [Image]
[18387|p651] Knowing the symmetry properties allows chemists to predict or explain the properties of molecules using a branch of mathematics called group theory.
[18388|p651|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[18389|p651] You will deal with line symmetry on the Cartesian plane when you deal with reflections about a line in Chapter 23.

[18511|p657|T1] 19.2 Symmetry in three dimensions
[18512|p657] There are two types of symmetry in three-dimensional shapes:
[18513|p657] plane symmetry
[18514|p657] rotational symmetry.
[18515|p657|T1] Plane symmetry
[18516|p657] A plane is a flat surface. If you can cut a solid in half along a plane so that each half is the mirror image of the other, then the solid has plane symmetry.
[18517|p657] This diagram of a cuboid shows that it can be cut three different ways to make two identical halves.
[18518|p657] [Image]
[18519|p657] [Image]
[18520|p657] [Image]
[18521|p657] The shaded area on each diagram represents the plane of symmetry (this is where you would cut it).
[18522|p657] Can you see that a sphere technically has an infinite number of planes of symmetry? It is symmetrical about any plane that passes through its centre.

[18560|p659|T1] 19.3 Symmetry properties of circles
[18561|p659] A circle has line symmetry about any diameter and it has rotational symmetry about its centre. From these facts a number of results can be deduced:
[18562|p659] 1 The perpendicular bisector of a chord passes through the centre.
[18563|p659] 2 Equal chords are equidistant from the centre, and chords equidistant from the centre are equal in length.
[18564|p659] 3 Two tangents drawn to a circle from the same point outside the circle are equal in length.
[18565|p659|T1] LINK
[18566|p659] The Earth rotates about an axis of symmetry. Where the Earth's axis meets the Earth's surface you will find the Geographical North and South Poles.
[18567|p660|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[18568|p660] Make sure you remember the correct names of circle parts.
[18569|p660] These were covered in Chapter 3 and revised in the Getting started activities in this chapter.
[18570|p660] The perpendicular bisector of a chord passes through the centre
[18571|p660] The perpendicular bisector of chord [EQ] is the locus of points equidistant from [EQ] and [EQ] .
[18572|p660] But centre [EQ] is equidistant from [EQ] and [EQ] (OA and OB are radii of circle with centre [EQ] ).
[18573|p660] ... [EQ] must be on the perpendicular bisector of [EQ] .
[18574|p660] This result can be expressed in other ways:
[18575|p660] The perpendicular from the centre of a circle to a chord meets the chord at its midpoint.
[18576|p660] The line joining the centre of a circle to the midpoint of a chord is perpendicular to the chord.
[18577|p660] You can use this fact to find the lengths of chords and the lengths of sides of right-angled triangles drawn between the centre and the chord.
[18578|p660|T1] TIP
[18579|p660] When answering questions you must state all symmetry and angle properties 'formally'. Learn the statements as they appear throughout the coming pages and be prepared to write them out when you answer ...

[18667|p665|T1] The angle between the tangent and radius is [EQ]
[18668|p665] Look at the diagram carefully.
[18669|p665] A tangent touches a circle at one point only.
[18670|p665] If you draw lines from the centre to points on the tangent, you can see that the shortest possible line is a radius. (OA).
[18671|p665] You already know that the shortest distance from a point to a line is the perpendicular distance from the point to the line.
[18672|p665] So, the radius is perpendicular to the tangent.
[18673|p665] It follows that angles [EQ] and [EQ] are right angles.

[18777|p669|T1] Alternate segment theorem
[18778|p669] You already know that where a tangent and diameter of a circle meet they form a right angle. The diameter will divide the circle into two semicircles.
[18779|p669] [Image]
[18780|p670] You also know that a diameter of any circle is a chord that passes through the centre of the circle. When a chord meets a tangent but does not pass through the centre, the circle is divided into a maj...
[18781|p670] [Image]
[18782|p670] You can draw two possible angles between the tangent and this chord. One crosses the major segment and the other crosses the minor segment.
[18783|p670] [Image]
[18784|p670] [Image]
[18785|p670] The segment that the angle does not cross is called the alternate segment. Draw an angle in the alternate segment.
[18786|p670] [Image]
[18787|p671|T1] INVESTIGATION
[18788|p671|T1] Understanding the alternate segment theorem
[18789|p671] 1 a Follow these instructions.
[18790|p671] Draw three large circles.
[18791|p671] - Draw a tangent to each circle, taking care to be as accurate as possible - your line should only touch the circle once.
[18792|p671] Draw a chord so that it meets the tangent.
[18793|p671] - Draw an angle between the tangent and chord. You can choose either angle.
[18794|p671] Work out which segment is the alternate segment and draw an angle in it.
[18795|p671] Measure, as accurately as you can, both the angle in the alternate segment and the angle between the tangent and the chord.
[18796|p671] b What do you notice?
[18797|p671] The alternate segment theorem states that the angle between the tangent and chord is always equal to the angle in the alternate segment. The diagram shows which angles are equal to which:
[18798|p671] [Image]
[18799|p671] 2 How do you prove the alternate segment theorem?
[18800|p671] This is an interesting proof because it will use some of the theorems that you already know.
[18801|p671] Begin by drawing the circle, chord, tangent, angle and angle in the alternate segment. Call the angles [EQ] and [EQ] respectively and label the point where the chord meets the tangent [EQ] .
[18802|p671] [Image]
[18803|p672|T1] INVESTIGATION CONTINUED
[18804|p672] Draw in lines PA and AB as indicated on this diagram. Note that PA goes through the centre of the circle.

[18809|p672] d Use the angle between a diameter and a tangent to show that [EQ]
[18810|p672] Now that you have seen how the theorem works, you can apply it like the others you know to solve problems.

[18836|p675|T1] SUMMARY
[18837|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>Do you know ...?</td></tr>
[18838|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>Shapes can have more than one line of symmetry. The number of lines of symmetry of a regular polygon corresponds with the number of sides the shape has.</td></tr>
[18839|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>The order of rotational symmetry tells you the number of times a shape fits onto itself in one rotation.</td></tr>
[18840|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>When a shape can be cut along a plane to form two solid parts that are mirror images of each other then it has plane symmetry.</td></tr>
[18841|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>If a three-dimensional shape is rotated around an axis and it looks the same at one or more positions during a complete revolution then it has rotational symmetry.</td></tr>
[18842|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>The perpendicular bisector of a chord passes through the centre of a circle.</td></tr>
[18843|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>Equal chords are equidistant from the centre and chords equidistant from the centre are equal in length.</td></tr>
[18844|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>Two tangents drawn to a circle from a point outside the circle are equal in length.</td></tr>
[18845|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>The angle in a semi-circle is a right angle.</td></tr>
[18846|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>The angle between a tangent and the radius of a circle is a right angle.</td></tr>
[18847|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>The angle subtended at the centre of a circle by an arc is twice the angle subtended at the circumference by the arc.</td></tr>
[18848|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>Angles in the same segment, subtended by the same arc, are equal.</td></tr>
[18849|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>Opposite angles of a cyclic quadrilateral add up to 180°.</td></tr>
[18850|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>Each exterior angle of a cyclic quadrilateral is equal to the interior angle opposite to it.</td></tr>
[18851|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>The alternate segment theorem states that the angle between the tangent and chord is always equal to the angle in the alternate segment.</td></tr>
[18852|p675|T1] Are you able to ...?
[18853|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>recognise rotational and line symmetry in two-dimensional shapes</td></tr>

[18855|p675] [Table Row] <tr><td>recognise rotational and line symmetry in three-dimensional shapes</td></tr>

[18962|p681|T1] 20.1 Histograms
[18963|p681] A histogram shows the distribution of continuous, grouped data. Look at this histogram showing the ages of adult patients admitted to hospital with a diagnosis of pneumonia.
[18964|p681] [Image]
[18965|p681] In this case, the class interval is 8 years. The shape of the graph shows that most patients admitted to hospital for pneumonia tend to be older (70 years or more) and that fewer middle-aged adults ar...
[18966|p681|T1] LINK
[18967|p681] Histograms are one of the most useful tools in epidemiology. They are used to organise and display a large set of measurements or numerical data in a simple way, so they are often used to display data...
[18968|p682] This histogram shows data grouped in unequal intervals. You can see that the first interval [EQ] is much larger than the others.
[18969|p682] [Image]
[18970|p682] On a histogram with unequal class intervals:
[18971|p682] - The horizontal scale is continuous and each column of the graph is drawn above a particular class interval.
[18972|p682] The frequency of the data is shown by the area of the bars. Frequency density is shown against the vertical axis.
[18973|p682|T1] Working with unequal class intervals
[18974|p682] When the class intervals are not equal you cannot use the height of each bar to work out the frequency. Instead, you need to use the area of each bar.
[18975|p682] When the class intervals are unequal you use the vertical scale to show the frequency density:
[18976|p682] [Equation]
[18977|p682] Frequency density takes into account the frequency relative to the size of the class interval and is used for comparing data in different-sized intervals.
[18978|p682|T1] LINK
[18979|p682] Density is used to express how much of a substance there is in a particular volume or area. For example, population density tells you how many people there are per square kilometre and the density of ...
[18980|p682|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[18981|p682] You worked with grouped data in Chapter 4.
[18982|p682|T1] TIP
[18983|p682] By rearranging the formula, you can see that frequency is equal to the area of a bar. You can use this to help you read frequencies from the histogram. Many questions are based on this principle.
[18984|p682|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 1
[18985|p682] Here is a table showing the heights of 25 plants. Draw a histogram to show these results.
[18986|p682] [Table Row] <tr><td>Height in cm</td><td>Number of plants</td></tr>
[18987|p682] [Table Row] <tr><td>5 ≤ h ≤ 15</td><td>4</td></tr>
[18988|p682] [Table Row] <tr><td>15 &lt; h ≤ 20</td><td>8</td></tr>
[18989|p682] [Table Row] <tr><td>20 &lt; h ≤ 25</td><td>7</td></tr>
[18990|p682] [Table Row] <tr><td>25 &lt; h ≤ 40</td><td>6</td></tr>
[18991|p683|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 1 CONTINUED
[18992|p683|T1] Answer
[18993|p683] First work out the frequency density by adding columns to your frequency distribution table like this:
[18994|p683] [Table Row] <tr><td>Height (h) in cm</td><td>Number of plants</td><td>Class width</td><td>Frequency density (frequency + class width)</td></tr>
[18995|p683] [Table Row] <tr><td>5 ≤ h ≤ 15</td><td>4</td><td>10</td><td>4/10 = 0.4</td></tr>
[18996|p683] [Table Row] <tr><td>15 &lt; h ≤ 20</td><td>8</td><td>5</td><td>8/5 = 1.6</td></tr>
[18997|p683] [Table Row] <tr><td>20 &lt; h ≤ 25</td><td>7</td><td>5</td><td>7/5 = 1.4</td></tr>
[18998|p683] [Table Row] <tr><td>25 &lt; h ≤ 40</td><td>6</td><td>15</td><td>6/15 = 0.4</td></tr>
[18999|p683] Next draw the axes. You will need to decide on a suitable scale for both the horizontal and the vertical axes.
[19000|p683] Once you have done this, draw the histogram, paying careful attention to the scales on the axes.
[19001|p683] [Image]
[19002|p683|T1] TIP
[19003|p683] If you plotted the data against frequency instead of frequency density (see histogram), it looks like there are more plants in the 25-40 class compared to the 5-10 class but actually, their frequency ...
[19004|p683] [Image]
[19005|p683] interval is misleading here, so we use frequency density as it is a better way to compare frequencies in classes of different sizes.
[19006|p683|T1] TIP
[19007|p683] The heights in cm are the class intervals. The number of plants is the frequency.

[19117|p688] Sometimes you may be asked questions such as:

[19119|p688] How many cars were travelling above [EQ]
[19120|p688] How many students scored less than [EQ] on the test?
[19121|p689] In statistics you can use a cumulative frequency table or a cumulative frequency curve to answer questions about data up to a particular class boundary. You can also use the cumulative frequencies to ...
[19122|p689|T1] Cumulative frequency tables
[19123|p689] Cumulative frequency is a 'running total' of the scores or results (the frequency in each group). The cumulative frequency gives the number of results which are less than, or equal to, a particular cl...
[19124|p689|T1] TIP
[19125|p689] Cumulative means increasing as more is added'. In daily use we might use the word 'accumulating' to mean the same thing.
[19126|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>Score out of 10</td><td>Frequency (f)</td><td>Cumulative frequency</td></tr>

[19129|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>5</td><td>3</td><td>9 + 3 = 12</td></tr>
[19130|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>6</td><td>3</td><td>12 + 3 = 15</td></tr>
[19131|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>7</td><td>5</td><td>20</td></tr>
[19132|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>27</td></tr>
[19133|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>9</td><td>2</td><td>29</td></tr>
[19134|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>10</td><td>1</td><td>30</td></tr>
[19135|p689] [Table Row] <tr><td>Total</td><td>30</td><td></td></tr>
[19136|p689] Each entry in the cumulative frequency column is calculated by adding the frequency of the current class to the previous cumulative frequency (or by adding all the frequencies up to and including the ...
[19137|p689] The last figure in the cumulative frequency column must equal the sum of the frequencies because all results will be below or equal to the highest result.

[19159|p690|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[19160|p690] Cumulative frequency curves allow you to estimate the median when there is a large number of data and you don't want to arrange the raw data in order.
[19161|p690|T1] Cumulative frequency curves
[19162|p690] When you plot the cumulative frequencies against the upper boundaries of each class interval you get a cumulative frequency curve. Cumulative frequency curves can be used to estimate other values.
[19163|p690] Cumulative frequency curves are also called ogive curves or ogives because they take the shape of narrow pointed arches called ogees.

[19274|p695|T1] Quartiles
[19275|p695] In Chapter 12 you found the range (the biggest value - the smallest value) to see how dispersed various sets of data were. However, the range is easily affected by outliers (extreme or unusual values)...
[19276|p695] You can divide the data shown on a cumulative frequency curve into quartiles to find the interquartile range, which is more representative than the range because it is not affected by extreme values.
[19277|p695] This cumulative frequency curve the marks obtained by 64 students in a test.
[19278|p695] [Image]
[19279|p695] When finding the positions of the quartiles from a cumulative frequency curve you do not use the [EQ] and [EQ] rules that you met for discrete data in
[19280|p695] Chapter 12. Instead, you use: [EQ] and [EQ] .
[19281|p695] Looking at the curve, you can see that:
[19282|p695] 48 students scored less than 15 marks. 15 marks is the upper quartile or third quartile [EQ] .
[19283|p695] 32 students scored less than 13 marks. 13 marks is the second quartile [EQ] , or median mark.
[19284|p695] 16 students scored less than 11 marks. 11 marks is the lower quartile or first quartile [EQ]
[19285|p695|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[19286|p695] Revise the work you did on quartiles and the interquartile range in Chapter 12 if you need to.
[19287|p695|T1] TIP
[19288|p695] Describing how 'dispersed' data is, is the same as describing how 'spread out' it is. So we can say that the range is a 'measure of dispersion' or a 'measure of spread'.
[19289|p695|T1] TIP
[19290|p695] Whole number values are being used in this example to make it easier to understand. Usually your answers will be estimates and they will involve decimal fractions.
[19291|p696|T1] The interquartile range
[19292|p696] The interquartile range (IQR) is the difference between the upper and lower quartiles: [EQ] .
[19293|p696] In effect, this is the range of the middle [EQ] of the scores, or the median of the upper half of the values minus the median of the lower half of the values.
[19294|p696] In the example about marks scored by students, the IQR [EQ]
[19295|p696] Because the interquartile range does not use any extreme small or large values it is considered a more reliable measure of spread than the range.

[19328|p697|T1] TIP

[19374|p700|T1] SUMMARY
[19375|p700] Do you know ...?
[19376|p700] Histograms are specialised bar graphs used for displaying continuous and grouped data.
[19377|p700] If the class widths are unequal, the bars are not equally wide and the vertical axis shows the frequency density.
[19378|p700] frequency per class interval Frequency density [EQ] class width
[19379|p700] Cumulative frequency is a running total of the class frequencies up to each upper class boundary.
[19380|p700] When cumulative frequencies are plotted they give a cumulative frequency curve or ogive.
[19381|p700] The curve can be used to estimate the median value in the data.
[19382|p700] The data can be divided into four equal groups called quartiles. The interquartile range is the difference between the upper and lower quartiles [EQ] .
[19383|p700] Large amounts of data can be divided into percentiles, which divide the data into 100 equal groups. If a score of 20 out of 25 is at the 82nd percentile it means that [EQ] of the scores are below 20.
[19384|p701|T1] SUMMARY CONTINUED

[19388|p701] calculate cumulative frequencies
[19389|p701] plot and draw a cumulative frequency curve
[19390|p701] use a cumulative frequency curve to estimate the median
[19391|p701] find quartiles and calculate the interquartile range
[19392|p701] estimate and interpret percentiles.

[19680|p716|T1] Attractive tablecloths
[19681|p716] A café owner has designed tablecloths for Monday to Friday. Each cloth has as many colours as possible but they must have some symmetry.
[19682|p716] The 5 by 5 tablecloths below each satisfy a different symmetry rule.
[19683|p716] Monday's 5 by 5 tablecloth has just 1 vertical line of symmetry.
[19684|p716] Design some tablecloths of other odd by odd sizes with just 1 line of symmetry.
[19685|p716] Check you agree that a 7 by 7 tablecloth can have at most 28 colours.
[19686|p716] Can you find a way of working out the maximum number of different colours that can be used on an [EQ] by [EQ] tablecloth (where [EQ] is odd), following Monday's rule?
[19687|p716] Tuesday's 5 by 5 tablecloth has rotational symmetry of order 4, and no lines of symmetry.

[19756|p720|T1] 21.1 Working with ratio
[19757|p720] A ratio is a numerical comparison of amounts. The order in which you write the amounts is very important. For example, if there is one teacher for every 25 students in a school, then the ratio of teac...
[19758|p720] We normally write ratios in the form [EQ] , but when we work with them, it is sometimes useful to express the ratio as a fraction.
[19759|p720] Think about the teacher to student ratio 1:25. This means there is 1 teacher for every 25 students.
[19760|p720] You can write this as [EQ] . This fraction compares the number of teachers with the number of students. It means that there is [EQ] as many teachers as there are students.
[19761|p720] So, if there are 150 students you can use the fraction to work out how many teachers there are:
[19762|p720] [Equation]
[19763|p720] So, for 150 students there will be 6 teachers.
[19764|p720] 6 is equivalent to [EQ] and this matches the ratio 1:25.
[19765|p720] When you write two quantities as a ratio, you must make sure they are both in the same units. For example, the ratio of 20c to $1 is not 20:1, it is 20:100 because there are 100 cents in a dollar.
[19766|p720|T1] Writing ratios in simplest form
[19767|p720] Ratios are in their simplest form when you write them using the smallest whole numbers possible. The ratio of 20:100 above is not in its simplest form. You can simplify ratios in the same way that you...

[19923|p726|T1] Dividing a quantity in a given ratio
[19924|p726] Ratios can be used to divide or share quantities. There are two ways of solving problems like these.
[19925|p726] Method 1: find the value of one part. This is the unitary method.
[19926|p726] 1 Add the values in the ratio to find the total number of parts involved.
[19927|p726] 2 Divide the quantity by the total number of parts to find the quantity per part (the value of one part).
[19928|p726] 3 Multiply the values in the ratio by the quantity per part to find the value of each part.
[19929|p726] Method 2: express the shares as fractions. This is the ratio method.
[19930|p726] 1 Add the values in the ratio to find the total number of parts involved.
[19931|p726] 2 Express each part of the ratio as a fraction of the total parts.
[19932|p726] 3 Multiply the quantity by the fraction to find the value of each part.
[19933|p726|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 4
[19934|p726] Share $24 between Jess and Anne in the ratio 3:5.
[19935|p726|T1] Answer
[19936|p726] Method 1
[19937|p726] [Equation]
[19938|p726] There are 8 parts in the ratio.
[19939|p726] [Equation]
[19940|p726] This is the value of 1 part.
[19941|p726] [Equation]
[19942|p726] Jess gets 3 parts: [EQ]
[19943|p726] [Equation]
[19944|p726] Anne gets 5 parts: [EQ]
[19945|p726|T1] Method 2
[19946|p726] [Equation]
[19947|p726] There are 8 parts in the ratio.
[19948|p726] Jess gets [EQ] of [EQ]
[19949|p726] Express each part as a fraction of the total parts and multiply by the quantity.
[19950|p726] Anne gets 5/8 of [EQ]15

[19983|p728|T1] 21.2 Ratio and scale
[19984|p728] Scale drawings (maps and plans) and models such as the one of the Louvre Museum in Abu Dhabi (shown at the start of this chapter), are the same shape as the real objects but they are generally smaller...
[19985|p728] Scale is a ratio. It can be expressed as 'length on drawing: real length'.
[19986|p728] The scale of a map, plan or model is usually given as a ratio in the form [EQ] . For example, the architects who designed the new Louvre building for Abu Dhabi made a [EQ] wide scale model of the dome...
[19987|p728] A scale of [EQ] means that a unit of measurement on the model must be multiplied by 33 to get the length (in the same units) of the real building. So, if the diameter of the dome in the model was [EQ]...
[19988|p728|T1] Expressing a ratio in the form [EQ]
[19989|p728] All ratio scales must be expressed in the form [EQ] or [EQ]
[19990|p728] To change a ratio so that one part [EQ] you need to divide both parts by the number that you want expressed as 1.

[20016|p729|T1] Solving scale problems
[20017|p729] There are two main types of problems involving scale:
[20018|p729] - calculating the real length of an object given its scaled length
[20019|p729] - calculating the scaled length of an object given its real length.
[20020|p729] In both cases you will be told one of the lengths and the scale, expressed as 'length on the diagram: real length'.
[20021|p729] You can write the scale as fractions and cross-multiply to find the unknown length.
[20022|p729|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 8
[20023|p729] The scale of a map is 1:25000.
[20024|p729] a What is real distance between two points that are [EQ] apart on the diagram?

[20026|p729|T1] Answers
[20027|p729] a Map distance: real distance [EQ]
[20028|p729] [Equation]
[20029|p729] [Equation]
[20030|p729] b [EQ]
[20031|p729] [Equation]
[20032|p729] Substitute the map distance and use [EQ] to represent the unknown real distance.
[20033|p729] Write the ratios as fractions.
[20034|p729] Cross multiply to find the real distance.
[20035|p729] The units in your answer will the same units as the map distance units.
[20036|p729] From part (a) you know the real distance is [EQ]
[20037|p729] You know that [EQ]
[20038|p729] So convert the real distance to km.

[20129|p733|T1] 21.3 Rates
[20130|p733] A rate compares two different quantities. In a rate, the quantity of one thing is usually given in relation to one unit of the other thing. For example, [EQ] per bottle, [EQ] or 100 people per square ...
[20131|p733] Rates can be simplified just like ratios. When a rate is in simplest form, the first quantity is expressed per one unit of the second, so, for example, [EQ] per hour is in simplest form, but [EQ] per ...
[20132|p733|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 11
[20133|p733] 492 people live in an area of [EQ] . Express this as a rate in its simplest terms.
[20134|p733|T1] Answer
[20135|p733] 492 people in [EQ]
[20136|p733] 492 people per km²
[20137|p733] [EQ] people/km²
[20138|p733] Divide by 12 to get a rate per unit.
[20139|p733] Don't forget to write the units.

[20229|p737|T1] 21.4 Kinematic graphs
[20230|p737] Kinematics is the study of how objects move. A kinematic graph may show how far an object moves over time or how its speed changes with time.
[20231|p737|T1] Distance-time graphs
[20232|p737] Graphs that show the connection between the distance an object has travelled and the time taken to travel that distance are called distance-time graphs or travel graphs.
[20233|p737] On such graphs, time is normally shown along the horizontal axis and distance on the vertical axis.
[20234|p737] [Image]
[20235|p737|T1] TIP
[20236|p737] In travel graphs time is an independent variable and it is always shown on the [EQ] -axis.
[20237|p737] Look at the graph. Can you see that it shows the following journey:
[20238|p737] a cycle ride for 4 minutes from home to a bus stop 1 km away
[20239|p737] a 2 minute wait for the bus
[20240|p737] a 7 km journey on the bus that takes 10 minutes.
[20241|p737] The line of the graph remains horizontal while the person is waiting for the bus because they don't get any further from the starting point. The steeper the line, the faster the person is travelling.
[20242|p737|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[20243|p737] You learned how to read and draw line graphs in Chapter 4.

[20281|p739|T1] Speed in distance-time graphs
[20282|p739] The steepness gradient of a graph gives an indication of speed.
[20283|p739] - A straight line on a graph indicates a constant speed. Any change in the angle of the line indicates a change of speed.
[20284|p739] [Image]
[20285|p740] The steeper the graph, the greater the speed.
[20286|p740] An upward slope and a downward slope represent movement in opposite directions.
[20287|p740] The distance-time graph shown is for a person who walks, cycles and then drives for three equal periods of time. For each period, speed is given by the formula:
[20288|p740] [Equation]
[20289|p740] [Image]
[20290|p740] Speed is a rate of change. A straight line from the origin shows a constant rate of change and you can use the formula [EQ] to find the rate of change.
[20291|p740] If the gradient changes along the graph, the speed will be different for different time periods.
[20292|p740] The gradient precisely describes the steepness of a line.
[20293|p740] In the diagram, the steepness of line [EQ] is measured by: increase in y-coordinate increase in x-coordinate
[20294|p740] This is the same as [EQ] or gradient of a straight line graph.
[20295|p740] A positive gradient means that as [EQ] increases [EQ] also increases.
[20296|p740] On a distance-time graph, the [EQ] value indicates distance from the starting point, so a positive gradient means the object is moving away from the starting point. A zero gradient means the [EQ] -val...
[20297|p740] For a distance-time graph:
[20298|p740] [Equation]
[20299|p740] Therefore, the gradient of the graph tells you the speed of the object and its direction of motion. This is known as the velocity of the object.
[20300|p740] [Image]
[20301|p741] Here is another example:
[20302|p741] The travel graph represents a car journey.
[20303|p741] The horizontal sections have zero gradient (so the car was stationary during these times).
[20304|p741] For section [EQ] , the gradient is positive:
[20305|p741] [Equation]
[20306|p741] the velocity was [EQ] in the direction away from home.
[20307|p741] For section [EQ] , the gradient is negative:
[20308|p741] [Equation]
[20309|p741] ..the velocity was [EQ]
[20310|p741] For section [EQ] the gradient is negative:
[20311|p741] [Equation]
[20312|p741] the velocity was [EQ]
[20313|p741] [Image]
[20314|p741|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[20315|p741] You learned how to calculate the gradient of a straight line graph in Chapter 10. Remember the gradient of a line is:
[20316|p741] positive if the line slopes up from left to right
[20317|p741] negative if the line slopes down from left to right.
[20318|p741|T1] TIP
[20319|p741] When the velocity is positive, the direction of motion is away from home. When the velocity is negative, the direction is towards home.

[20367|p744|T1] Speed-time graphs
[20368|p744] In certain cases, the speed (or velocity) of an object may change. An increase in speed is called acceleration; a decrease in speed is called deceleration. A speed-time graph shows speed (rather than ...
[20369|p744] This graph shows a train journey between two stations.
[20370|p744] [Image]
[20371|p744] The train starts at zero speed.
[20372|p744] The speed increases steadily reaching [EQ] after 15 seconds.
[20373|p744] The train travels at a constant speed (horizontal section) of [EQ] for 25 seconds.
[20374|p744] The train then slows down at a steady rate till it stops.
[20375|p744] The entire journey takes 60 seconds.
[20376|p744] Look at the first part of the journey again.
[20377|p744] The speed increased by [EQ] in 15 seconds.
[20378|p744] [EQ] is the gradient of the line representing the first part of the journey.
[20379|p744] This is a rate of [EQ] every second. This is the rate of acceleration. You write this as [EQ] (or [EQ]
[20380|p744] For a speed-time graph, the gradient = acceleration.
[20381|p744] A positive gradient (acceleration) is an increase in speed.
[20382|p744] A negative gradient (deceleration) is a decrease in speed.
[20383|p744|T1] Distance travelled in a speed-time graph
[20384|p744] You already know that distance = speed × time. On a speed-time graph, this is represented by the area of shapes under the sections of graph. You can use the graph to work out the distance travelled.
[20385|p744|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[20386|p744] You worked with distance, time and speed earlier in this chapter. Make sure you remember how to use and manipulate the formulae.
[20387|p744|T1] TIP
[20388|p744] You may sometimes see m/s written as m s-1 and m/s2 written as m s-2.
[20389|p745|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 16
[20390|p745] This speed-time graph represents the motion of a particle over a period of five seconds.
[20391|p745] [Image]
[20392|p745] a During which periods of time was the particle accelerating?
[20393|p745] b Calculate the particle's acceleration 3 seconds after the start.

[20395|p745|T1] Answers
[20396|p745] a The particle was accelerating in the period 0 to 0.8 seconds (section OA) and in the period 2 to 3.5 seconds (section BC).
[20397|p745] b The gradient is constant between 2 and 3.5 seconds so acceleration was also constant in that period. So, the acceleration 3 seconds after the start is:
[20398|p745] [Equation]
[20399|p745] c Distance travelled [EQ] area under graph
[20400|p745] [Equation]

[20446|p747|T1] 21.5 Proportion
[20447|p747] Quantities increase or decrease in proportion if multiplying (or dividing) one quantity by a value results in multiplying (or dividing) the other quantity by the same value. In other words, there is a...
[20448|p747|T1] Direct proportion
[20449|p747] When two quantities are in direct proportion they increase or decrease at the same rate. In other words, the ratio of the quantities is equivalent (in general terms, [EQ] ). If there is an increase or...
[20450|p747|T1] LINK
[20451|p747] Home economists, chefs and food technologists use proportional reasoning to mix ingredients, convert between units or work out the cost of a dish.
[20452|p748] Here are some examples of quantities that are in direct proportion:
[20453|p748] [Table Row] <tr><td>Speed (km/h)</td><td>0</td><td>45</td><td>60</td><td>75</td><td>90</td><td>120</td></tr>
[20454|p748] [Table Row] <tr><td>Distance covered in an hour (km)</td><td>0</td><td>45</td><td>60</td><td>75</td><td>90</td><td>120</td></tr>
[20455|p748] Distance = speed × time, so the faster you drive in a set time, the further you will travel in that time.
[20456|p748] [Table Row] <tr><td>Number of items</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td></tr>
[20457|p748] [Table Row] <tr><td>Mass (kg)</td><td>0</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>6</td><td>8</td></tr>
[20458|p748] If one item has a mass of [EQ] , then two of the same item will have a mass of [EQ] and so on. The more you have of the same item, the greater the mass will be.
[20459|p748] [Table Row] <tr><td>Number of hours worked</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td></tr>
[20460|p748] [Table Row] <tr><td>Amount earned ($)</td><td>0</td><td>12</td><td>24</td><td>36</td></tr>
[20461|p748] The more hours you work, the more you earn.
[20462|p748|T1] Graphs of directly proportional relationships
[20463|p748] If you draw the graph of a directly proportional relationship you will get a straight line that passes through the origin.
[20464|p748] Of course, the converse (opposite) of this is also true. When a graph is a straight line that passes through the origin, one quantity is directly proportional to the other.

[20468|p748] When a liquid cools, the rate at which the temperature decreases is directly proportional to the difference between the temperature of the liquid and the temperature of the surroundings.
