[20700|p757|T1] SUMMARY
[20701|p757|T1] Do you know ...?
[20702|p757] A ratio is a comparison of two or more quantities in a set order. Ratios can be expressed in the form [EQ] or [EQ] . Ratios have no units.
[20703|p757] Ratios can be simplified by multiplying or dividing both quantities by the same number. This method produces equivalent ratios.
[20704|p757] Map scales are good examples of ratios in everyday life. The scale of a map is usually given in the form [EQ] . This allows you to convert map distances to real distances using the ratio scale.
[20705|p757] A rate is a comparison of two different quantities. Usually a rate gives an amount of one quantity per unit of the other. Rates must include the units of the quantities.
[20706|p757] Speed is one of the most common rates of change. Speed = distance ÷ time.
[20707|p757] Kinematic graphs are used to show relationships between:
[20708|p757] distance and time (distance-time graph)
[20709|p757] speed and time (speed-time graph)
[20710|p757] and to solve problems in these areas.
[20711|p757|T1] TIP
[20712|p757] Sometimes you may see 'varies as' written instead of 'is directly proportional to'. Similarly, 'varies inversely with' means 'is inversely proportional to'.
[20713|p758|T1] SUMMARY CONTINUED
[20714|p758] The gradient of a distance-time graph shows how the speed changes over time.
[20715|p758] Proportion is a constant ratio between two sets of numbers.
[20716|p758] When quantities are in direct proportion they increase or decrease at the same rate.
[20717|p758] When quantities are inversely proportional, one increases as the other decreases.
[20718|p758] Algebraic expressions can be used to represent direct and indirect (inverse) proportion and to solve problems related to these concepts. The symbol for proportion is [EQ] .
[20719|p758|T1] Are you able to ...?
[20720|p758] To simplify ratios and find the missing values in equivalent ratios,
[20721|p758] divide quantities in a given ratio
[20722|p758] convert measurements on maps, plans and other scale diagrams to real measurements and vice versa
[20723|p758] express relationships between different quantities as rates in their simplest form and solve problems relating to rates
[20724|p758] read and interpret kinematic graphs
[20725|p758] by calculating average speed
[20726|p758] by calculating acceleration and deceleration from a graph and finding the distance travelled using the area under a linear speed-time graph
[20727|p758] 1. solve problems involving direct and indirect proportion
[20728|p758] express direct and inverse proportion in algebraic terms
[20729|p758] solve direct and inverse proportion problems using algebraic methods.

[21010|p771|T1] 22.2 Using and rearranging formulae
[21011|p771] You already know that the variable which is written alone on one side of the [EQ] sign (usually the left) of a formula is called the subject of the formula. For example, in the formula for finding the...
[21012|p771] In some situations it is more helpful to have a different variable as the subject, so you will need to rearrange the formula.
[21013|p771] To change the subject of a formula:
[21014|p771] expand to get rid of any brackets if necessary
[21015|p771] use inverse operations to isolate the variable required.
[21016|p772|T1] WORKED EXAMPLE 6
[21017|p772] Solve for [EQ] if [EQ]
[21018|p772] Answer
[21019|p772] [Equation]

[21251|p779|T1] Domain and range
[21252|p779] A function is defined by a rule that provides the output values for corresponding input values.
[21253|p779] The domain (D) of a function is the set of input values. These are the possible values for the independent variable (the [EQ] -values).
[21254|p779] The range (R) is the set of output values from the rule. These are the [EQ] -values, which are dependent on the variable input.
[21255|p779] There may be restrictions on the domain. For example, if you are working with time or distance, the input values cannot be negative. Restrictions on the domain may be given with the function. For exam...
[21256|p779|T1] TIP
[21257|p779|T1] Remember:
[21258|p779] You cannot divide by 0.
[21259|p779] The square root of a negative number is not a real number.
[21260|p779] The square root sign [EQ] refers to the positive square root only.

[21425|p783|T1] Inverse functions
[21426|p783] The inverse of any function (f) is the function that will do the opposite of f. In other words, the function that will undo the effects of f. So, if f maps 4 onto 13, then the inverse of f will map 13...
[21427|p783] In effect, when [EQ] is applied to a number and the inverse of [EQ] is applied to the result, you will get back to the number you started with.

[21429|p783] Similarly, the inverse of [EQ] is [EQ] , because to undo multiply by two you have to divide by two.

[21434|p784|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[21435|p784] You briefly worked with inverse functions in trigonometry in Chapter 15.
[21436|p784|T1] Finding the inverse of a function
[21437|p784] There are two methods of finding the inverse:
[21438|p784] Method 1: using a flow diagram.
[21439|p784] In this method you draw a flow diagram for the function and then work out the inverse by 'reversing' the flow to undo the operations in the boxes.
[21440|p784] Method 2: reversing the mapping.
[21441|p784] In this method you use the fact that if [EQ] maps [EQ] onto [EQ] , then [EQ] maps [EQ] onto [EQ] .
[21442|p784] To find [EQ] you have to find a value of [EQ] that corresponds to a given value of [EQ] .
[21443|p784] Worked examples 17-20 show how to find the inverse for two functions, first using method 1 and then using method 2.

[21524|p786|T1] SUMMARY
[21525|p786|T1] Do you know ...?
[21526|p786] When you set up your own equations to represent problems you need to state what the variables stand for.
[21527|p786] You can rearrange formulae to make any of the variables the subject. This is called changing the subject of the formula. It may also be called solving the formula for [EQ] or expressing the formula in...
[21528|p786] A function is a rule for changing one variable into another.
[21529|p786] Functions are written using notation of [EQ] .
[21530|p786] You can use a flow diagram to represent the steps in a function.
[21531|p786] A composite function is a function of a function. The order of a composite function is important [EQ] means do g first then f.
[21532|p786] An inverse function is function that undoes the original function. It is the reverse of the function.
[21533|p786|T1] Are you able to ...?
[21534|p786] set up your own equations and use them to solve worded problems
[21535|p786|T1] change the subject of formula
[21536|p786] set up and rearrange more complicated formulae such as those that contain squares, square roots or where the subject appears in more than one term
[21537|p786] read, understand and use function notation to describe simple functions
[21538|p786] form composite functions such as [EQ] and [EQ]
[21539|p786] find the inverse of a function using a flow diagram
[21540|p786] find the inverse of a function by reversing the mapping.

[21649|p791|T1] 23.1 Simple plane transformations
[21650|p791] Transformation means change. In mathematics, a transformation is a change in the position or size of an object (or point). In this section you will deal with four types of transformations:
[21651|p791] - reflection (a flip or mirror image)
[21652|p791] rotation (a turn)
[21653|p791] translation (a slide movement)
[21654|p791] enlargement (making the object larger or smaller).
[21655|p791] REFLECTION

[21661|p791] A transformation produces an image of the original object in a new position or at a different size. A point, [EQ] , on the object is labelled as [EQ] on the image.
[21662|p791] Reflections, rotations and translations change the position of an object, but they do not change its size. So, the object and its image are congruent. If you place the object and its image on top of e...
[21663|p791] When you enlarge an object, you change its size. The object and its image are similar. In other words, the lengths of corresponding sides on the image are in the same proportion as on the object and t...

[21674|p792|T1] Properties of reflection
[21675|p792] A point and its image are equidistant from the mirror line [EQ] after reflection about the line [EQ]
[21676|p792] The mirror line bisects the line joining a point and its image at right angles.
[21677|p792] A line segment and its image are equal in length. [EQ]
[21678|p792] A line and its image are equally inclined to the mirror line. angle [EQ] angle [EQ]
[21679|p792] - Points on the mirror line are their own images and are invariant.
[21680|p792] Under reflection, a figure and its image are congruent.

[21748|p795|T1] Rotation
[21749|p795] A rotation is a turn around a fixed point. Rotation occurs when an object is turned around a given point. Rotation can be clockwise or anti-clockwise. The fixed point is called the centre of rotation ...
[21750|p795] In this diagram, the object has been rotated [EQ] clockwise about the centre of rotation (a vertex of the object).
[21751|p795] [Image]
[21752|p796|T1] Properties of rotation
[21753|p796] A rotation through [EQ] is a half turn; a rotation through [EQ] is a quarter turn.
[21754|p796] A point and its image are equidistant from the centre of rotation.
[21755|p796] Each point of an object moves along the arc of a circle whose centre is the centre of rotation. All the circles are concentric:
[21756|p796] [Image]
[21757|p796] Only the centre of rotation is invariant.
[21758|p796] The object and the image are congruent after rotation.
[21759|p796] [Image]
[21760|p796] The perpendicular bisector of a line joining a point and its image passes through the centre of rotation.
[21761|p796] [Image]
[21762|p796] A line segment and its image are equal in length.
[21763|p796] [Image]
[21764|p796|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[21765|p796] You dealt with rotation when you studied rotational symmetry in Chapter 19.
[21766|p796] [Image]
[21767|p797] To describe a rotation you need to give:
[21768|p797] the centre of rotation
[21769|p797] the amount of turn [EQ] or [EQ]
[21770|p797] the direction of the turn (clockwise or anti-clockwise).
[21771|p797] You can use thin paper or tracing paper to help you with rotations:
[21772|p797] 1 Trace the shape and label the vertices.
[21773|p797] 2 Place the tracing over the object.
[21774|p797] 3 Use the point of a pair of compasses or pen to hold the paper at the point (centre) of rotation.
[21775|p797] 4 Turn the paper through the given turn.
[21776|p797] 5 The new position of the shape is the image.
[21777|p797|T1] TIP
[21778|p797] The centre of rotation will normally be the origin (0, 0), a vertex of the shape or the midpoint of a side of the shape. The amount of turn will normally be a multiple of [EQ] .

[21816|p799|T1] Translation
[21817|p799] A translation, or slide, is the movement of an object over a specified distance along a line. The object is not twisted or turned. The movement is indicated by positive or negative signs according to ...
[21818|p800] You can describe a translation using a column vector: [EQ] . This means a movement of [EQ] units in the [EQ] -direction (left or right) and a movement of [EQ] units in the [EQ] -direction (up or down)...

[21828|p800|T1] Properties of translation
[21829|p800] A translation moves every point in an object the same distance in the same direction, so you can describe the translation of the whole object by describing the translation of any one point.
[21830|p800] To specify the translation, give both the distance and direction of the translation using a column vector [EQ] .
[21831|p800] No part of the object is invariant.
[21832|p800] The object and the image are congruent.
[21833|p800|T1] TIP

[21865|p802|T1] Enlargement
[21866|p802] When you enlarge a shape, you make it bigger (or smaller). In an enlargement the lengths of sides on the object are multiplied by a constant scale factor [EQ] to form the image. The sizes of angles do...
[21867|p802] [Equation]
[21868|p802] If the scale factor is given, you can find the lengths of corresponding sides by multiplication.

[21870|p802] This means that [EQ] .
[21871|p802] [Image]
[21872|p802] When an object is enlarged from a fixed point, it has a centre of enlargement. The centre of enlargement determines the position of the image. Lines drawn through corresponding points on the object an...
[21873|p802] You can determine the scale factor by comparing any two corresponding sides, for example:
[21874|p802] [Equation]
[21875|p802] or by comparing the distances of two corresponding vertices from the origin, for example:

[21878|p802|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[21879|p802] 'Scale factor' was introduced in Chapter 11. It is the multiplier that tells you how much one shape is larger than another.
[21880|p802|T1] LINK
[21881|p802] When you pinch an image on a phone screen to make it bigger you are transforming the image by enlargement.
[21882|p803|T1] Properties of enlargement
[21883|p803] The centre of enlargement can be anywhere (inside the object, outside the object or on a vertex or line).
[21884|p803] - A scale factor greater than 1 enlarges the object whilst a scale factor between 0 and 1 reduces the size of the object, although this is still described as an enlargement.
[21885|p803] An object and its image are similar (not congruent) with sides in the ratio [EQ] where [EQ] is the scale factor.
[21886|p803] - Angles of the object are invariant.
[21887|p803|T1] TIP
[21888|p803] Remember, similar shapes are the same shape but not the same size. Congruent shapes are the same size.

[22053|p817|T1] 23.3 Vectors
[22054|p817|T1] LINK
[22055|p817] The word 'vector' means carrier. In a mathematical context, it is about picking an object up and translating it. In a biological context, animals (including humans) that carry diseases are known as ve...
[22056|p817] Some quantities are best described by giving both a magnitude (size) and a direction. For example, a wind speed of [EQ] from the southeast or an acceleration upwards of [EQ] . Force, velocity, displac...
[22057|p817] Other quantities such as time, temperature, speed, mass and area can be described by only giving their magnitude (they don't have a direction). These quantities are called scalars.
[22058|p817] A vector is an ordered pair of numbers that you can use to describe a translation. The ordered pair gives both magnitude and direction.
[22059|p817|T1] Vector notation
[22060|p817] Vectors can be represented by a directed line segment as shown in the diagrams. Note that the notation is either a small letter with a wavy line beneath it or a bold letter: e.g. [EQ] or [EQ] .
[22061|p817] [Image]
[22062|p817] Vectors can also be represented by a named line such as [EQ] . In such cases, the vector is denoted by [EQ] . The order of letters is important because they give the direction of the line. [EQ] is not...
[22063|p817|T1] Writing vectors as number pairs
[22064|p817] Vectors can also be written as a column vector using number pair notation. Look at line [EQ] in the diagram.
[22065|p818] [Image]
[22066|p818] This line represents the translation of [EQ] to [EQ] . The translation is two units in the positive [EQ] -direction and four units in the positive [EQ] -direction. You can write this as the ordered pa...
[22067|p818] You can therefore write [EQ] .

[22120|p821|T1] Equal vectors
[22121|p821] Equal vectors have the same size (magnitude) and direction. As vectors are usually independent of position, they can start at any point. The same vector can be at many places in a diagram.
[22122|p821] In the diagram, [EQ] and [EQ] are equal vectors.
[22123|p821] [Image]
[22124|p821] [Equation]
[22125|p821] When equal vectors share a common point, for example when [EQ] you can deduce that that the points [EQ] and [EQ] all lie on a straight line. When points lie on a straight line, they are collinear.

[22427|p834|T1] SUMMARY
[22428|p834|T1] Do you know ...?
[22429|p834] A transformation involves a change in the position and/or size of a shape.
[22430|p834] A reflection is a mirror image, a rotation is a turn, a translation is a slide and an enlargement is an increase in size.
[22431|p834] To fully describe a reflection you need to give the equation of the mirror line.
[22432|p834] To fully describe a rotation you need to give the angle and centre of rotation.
[22433|p834] To describe a translation you can use a column vector [EQ] .
[22434|p834] To describe an enlargement you need to give the scale factor and the centre of enlargement.
[22435|p834] A vector has both magnitude and direction. You can add and subtract vectors but you cannot multiply or divide vectors. You can multiply a vector by a scalar.
[22436|p834] The magnitude of a vector [EQ] . You write the magnitude as [EQ] or [EQ] .
[22437|p834] A position vector is a vector that starts at the origin.
[22438|p834|T1] Are you able to ...?
[22439|p834] To reflect points and plane figures about horizontal and vertical lines,
[22440|p834] To rotate plane figures about the origin, vertices of the object and midpoints of the sides are
[22441|p834] translate shapes using a column vector
[22442|p834] to construct enlargements of simple shapes using the scale factor and centre of enlargement
[22443|p834] recognise and describe single transformations
[22444|p834] recognise and describe combined transformations
[22445|p834] add and subtract vectors and multiply vectors by a scalar
[22446|p834] calculate the magnitude of a vector
[22447|p834] use position vectors to find the magnitude of vectors.

[22513|p838|T1] IN THIS CHAPTER YOU WILL:

[22517|p839] The probability of getting heads when you toss one coin is 0.5. But what is the probability of getting heads only when you toss two, three or 20 coins at the same time?

[22519|p839] In this chapter, you are going to use diagrams to show possible outcomes and calculate the probability of combined events to deepen your understanding of probability concepts.

[22564|p841|T1] 24.2 Calculating probability from tree diagrams
[22565|p841|T1] MATHEMATICAL CONNECTIONS
[22566|p841] For independent events [EQ] and then [EQ]
[22567|p841] For mutually exclusive events [EQ] .
[22568|p841] Read through Chapter 8 again if you have forgotten this.
[22569|p841] Here is the tree diagram showing possible outcomes for throwing a dice and tossing a coin at the same time (H is used for head and T is used for tail).
[22570|p841] This is the same diagram as the one at the start of Section 24.1, but now the probability of each outcome is written by the side of each branch.
[22571|p841] [Image]
[22572|p842|T1] LINK
[22573|p842] One particular kind of study of tiny particles is called quantum mechanics. This looks at the probability of finding particles in particular places at particular times.
[22574|p842|T1] The probability of combined events on a tree diagram
[22575|p842] To find the probability of one particular combination of outcomes:
[22576|p842] - multiply the probabilities on consecutive branches, for example the probability of throwing a 5 and getting an H is [EQ] .
[22577|p842] To find the probability when there is more than one favourable combination or when the events are mutually exclusive:
[22578|p842] - multiply the probabilities on consecutive branches
[22579|p842] - add the probabilities (of each favourable combination) obtained by multiplication, for example, throwing 1 or 2 and getting an H is
[22580|p842] [Equation]
[22581|p842] When you are interested only in specific probabilities, you can draw a tree diagram that only shows the favourable outcomes. For example, if you wanted to find the probability of getting a number [EQ]...
[22582|p842] [Equation]

[22809|p851|T1] 24.4 Conditional probability
[22810|p851] If the probability of an event depends on the outcome of a previous event then the probability is said to be conditional.
[22811|p851] For two events [EQ] and [EQ] , [EQ] given that [EQ] has happened) refers to the conditional probability of [EQ] happening given that [EQ] has already happened.
[22812|p851] The way that you work out the conditional probability depends on whether the events are independent or not.
[22813|p851|T1] Independent events
[22814|p851] Imagine you roll two normal six-sided dice.
[22815|p851] The first dice lands on a 6. What is the probability of the second dice also landing on 6?
[22816|p851] These two events are independent. The score on the second dice is not affected by the outcome of the first.
[22817|p851] If two events [EQ] and [EQ] are independent, it is always true that
[22818|p851] [EQ] given that [EQ] has happened) [EQ]
[22819|p851] so in this case, the probability of getting a 6 with the second dice is [EQ] .
[22820|p851|T1] Dependent events
[22821|p851] If two events [EQ] and [EQ] are dependent, the first event does affect the probability of the second event.
[22822|p851] For example, suppose you have an apple, an orange and a banana and you choose two of the fruits at random. If you choose the apple first, you can only choose between the orange and the banana for your...
[22823|p851] To find the probability of [EQ] given that [EQ] has happened, use the rule:
[22824|p851] [Equation]
[22825|p851] When you deal with Venn diagrams, this rule can be written in set language as:
[22826|p851] [Equation]
[22827|p851] You can use tree diagrams and Venn diagrams to help you solve problems involving conditional probability.
[22828|p851|T1] TIP
[22829|p851] When you are using a tree diagram always check whether the probability of an event changes because of the outcome of a previous event. Questions involving conditional probability often contain the ins...

[22979|p858] [Table Row] <tr><td>Tree diagrams and Venn diagrams are useful for organising the outcomes of different stages in an event. They are particularly useful when there are more than two stages because a s...
[22980|p858] [Table Row] <tr><td>The outcomes are written at the ends of branches on a tree diagram. The probability of each outcome is written next to the branches as a fraction or decimal.</td></tr>
[22981|p858] [Table Row] <tr><td>For independent events you find the probability by multiplying the probabilities on each branch of the tree.</td></tr>
[22982|p858] [Table Row] <tr><td>When events are mutually exclusive, you need to add the probabilities obtained by multiplication.</td></tr>
[22983|p858] [Table Row] <tr><td>The probability that an event happens, given that another event has already happened, is called conditional probability.</td></tr>
[22984|p858] [Table Row] <tr><td>Are you able to ...?</td></tr>

[23360|p875|T1] > Glossary
[23361|p875|T1] Key words
[23362|p875] adjacent: a 'shorter' side of a right-angled triangle that is 'next to' an angle other than the right angle.
[23363|p875] alternate angles: a pair of angles on opposite sides of the transversal between two parallel lines.
[23364|p875] alternate segment: when an angle is drawn between a tangent and a chord the alternate segment is the segment that the angle does not cross.
[23365|p875] apex: in a pyramid, the apex is the point, above the base, at which all the sloping sides meet.
[23366|p875] asymptote: a line that a graph approaches but never intersects.
[23367|p875] axis of symmetry: a line that divides a plane shape into two symmetrical halves or a 'rod' about which a solid can rotate and still look the same in different positions.
[23368|p875] base: when working with indices, the base is the number that is being raised to a power.
[23369|p875] bearing: an angle indicating the direction of travel between two points. The bearing begins from the 'North' direction and is measured clockwise round to the line joining start point and destination.
[23370|p875] bias: something that affects the chance of an event occurring in favour of a desired outcome.
[23371|p875] bivariate data: two measurements, relating to an investigation, taken at the same time.
[23372|p875] categorical data: non-numerical data.
[23373|p875] centre of rotation: the point around which a plane shape can rotate and show the same shape in different positions.
[23374|p875] class interval: the group into which numeral data has been placed.
[23375|p875] coefficient: in a term which is a mixture of numbers and letters, the coefficient is the number that is multiplying the letters.
[23376|p875] co-interior angles: a pair of angles between the parallel lines on the same side of a transversal.
[23377|p875] column vector: number pair notation used to describe a vector as the movement between two points: [EQ] units in the [EQ] -direction (left or right) and [EQ] units in the in the [EQ] -direction (up or ...
[23378|p875] combined events: one event followed by another event.
[23379|p875] common denominator: a common value that two or more fractions need to be converted to in order to be able to add and/or subtract fractions.
[23380|p875] complement (of a set): set of objects that are not in a set, but which are part of the larger universal set.
[23381|p875] complementary angles: two angles that add to give [EQ]
[23382|p875] completing the square: writing a quadratic expression in the form [EQ] .
[23383|p875] composite function: applying a function to a value and then another function to that result.
[23384|p875] composite number: integer with more than two factors i.e. it has more factors than just 1 and itself.
[23385|p875] compound interest: interest paid on interest already earned and not just the original capital.
[23386|p875] conditional probability: the probability of an event, assuming that another has occurred.
[23387|p875] congruent: shapes that are identical in both shape and size.
[23388|p875] consecutive: following one after the other.
[23389|p875] constant term: a term in an equation or expression that has a fixed numerical value.
[23390|p875] Continuous data: data that can take any value in a range, such as height or weight.
[23391|p875] conversion: changing one quantity or unit into its equivalent in another unit.
[23392|p875] correlation: the relationship between bivariate data.
[23393|p875] corresponding angles: a pair of angles in the same position at each intersection.
[23394|p875] cosine ratio: for a given angle (other than the right-angle) in a right-angled triangle, the cosine ratio is the length of the side adjacent to the angle divided by the hypotenuse of the triangle.
[23395|p875] Cosine rule: a formula connecting the three sides of a triangle and one of the angles.
[23396|p875] cost price: the price that a trader pays for goods.
[23397|p875] cube number: a cube number is the result obtained when a number is multiplied by itself and then multiplied by itself again.
[23398|p875] cube root: the number which, when multiplied by itself, and then by itself again, gives the cube.
[23399|p875] Cumulative frequency: a 'running total' of the frequencies.
[23400|p876] cumulative frequency curve: a curve formed using the cumulative frequencies as the vertical axis value.
[23401|p876] cyclic quadrilateral: a quadrilateral whose vertices all exactly touch the circumference of a circle.
[23402|p876] denominator: the number on the bottom of a fraction.
[23403|p876] dependent variable: a variable whose value depends on the value of another variable.
[23404|p876] derivative of function: the derivative of function (or derivative) of a function is another function that gives the rate of change at any point on a graph.
[23405|p876] difference between two squares: a method of factorising (putting into brackets) one squared term subtracted from another.
[23406|p876] differentiation: the process of finding a derived function, which tells you the gradient of the function at a point on the curve.
[23407|p876] direct proportion: when two quantities increase or decrease at the same rate.
[23408|p876] discount: the amount by which an original selling price is reduced.
[23409|p876] discrete data: data that can only take certain (usually integer) values.
[23410|p876] element: an item in a set, sometimes called a member of the set.
[23411|p876] empty set: a set with no elements.
[23412|p876] enlargement: a transformation of a shape that keeps the ratio of corresponding sides the same but increases or decreases the lengths of the sides.
[23413|p876] Equation of a line: a formula that shows how the [EQ] -coordinate is related to the [EQ] -coordinate, for any point on a line.
[23414|p876] equivalent fraction: the result of multiplying (or dividing) the top and bottom of a fraction by the same value.
[23415|p876] event: the outcome that is being tested for in a probability 'experiment'.
[23416|p876] Exact value: precise or accurate value, not an approximation.
[23417|p876] expand: multiply the terms inside a bracket by the term multiplying the bracket. (This includes multiplying one bracket by another.)
[23418|p876] experimental probability: the chance of an event happening, calculated by running an 'experiment' many times.
[23419|p876] exponent: another word for power or index, indicating how many times a base number is multiplied by itself.
[23420|p876] exponential: a function formed when the variable is in the index.
[23421|p876] expression: a group of terms linked by operation signs.
[23422|p876] extrapolation: a value determined by continuing a line of best fit beyond the plotted data.
[23423|p876] face: a plane shape that forms part of a solid.
[23424|p876] factor: a number that divides exactly into another number with no remainder.
[23425|p876] factorisation: to rewrite an expression using brackets.
[23426|p876] formula: a general 'rule' expressed algebraically (such as how to find the area of a shape).
[23427|p876] frequency density: the frequency of a class divided by the width of the class.
[23428|p876] function: a set of rules or instructions for changing one number (an input) into another (an output).
[23429|p876] function notation: an alternative mathematical way of writing equations.
[23430|p876] Gradient: the steepness of a line (or the steepness of a tangent drawn at a point on a curve).
[23431|p876] grouped data: the collection of individual data values into convenient groups. Used especially for continuous data.
[23432|p876] histogram: a specialised graph used to illustrate grouped continuous data.
[23433|p876] hyperbola: a graph defined by the equation [EQ] , where [EQ] is a constant. (Also called reciprocal graphs.)
[23434|p876] hypotenuse: the longest side of a right angled triangle.
[23435|p876] independent events: events where the outcome is not affected/influenced by what has occurred before.
[23436|p876] index: another word for power or exponent, indicating how many times a base number is multiplied by itself.
[23437|p876] Index notation: a method of writing number using a base number and a power, for example [EQ] .
[23438|p876] integer: any of the negative and positive whole numbers, including zero.
[23439|p876] interest: the amount charged for borrowing, or earned for investing, money.
[23440|p876] Interest rate: the percentage charged for borrowing, or earned for investing, money. (Usually an annual rate.)
[23441|p876] interquartile range: the difference between the upper and lower quartiles.
[23442|p876] intersection: the intersection of two sets is the set of elements that are found in both sets (the overlapping or shared elements).
[23443|p876] inverse function: a function that does the opposite of the original function.
[23444|p876] inverse proportion: when one quantity decreases in the same proportion as another quantity increases.
[23445|p877] irrational number: a (decimal) number that does not terminate or recur and cannot be written as a fraction.
[23446|p877] line of best fit: a trend line drawn onto a scatter graph that passes as close to as many data points as possible.
[23447|p877] line symmetry: a line that divides a plane shape into two halves so that one half is the mirror image of the other. (See also axis of symmetry.)
[23448|p877] linear equation: a linear equation has no terms with a power in [EQ] greater than one.
[23449|p877] linear inequalities: similar to linear equations but using [EQ] or [EQ] .
[23450|p877] loss: when goods are sold for less than they were bought, the loss is the cost price less the selling price.
[23451|p877] lower bound: the exact smallest value that a number (given to a specified accuracy) could be.
[23452|p877] lower quartile: the value of data at the [EQ] percentile.
[23453|p877] lowest terms: an equivalent fraction where the numerator and denominator are the smallest allowable whole numbers. Also called simplest form.
[23454|p877] magnitude: the size (of a vector) irrespective of direction.
[23455|p877] maximum: a turning point or vertex on a graph whose y-coordinate is greater than points immediately to its left and right.
[23456|p877] mean: an average that uses all the data.
[23457|p877] median: an average, the middle value of data when it is arranged in increasing order.
[23458|p877] metric: the metric system uses measurements that are fractions or multiples of metres.
[23459|p877] midpoint: exactly halfway between the ends of a line segment.
[23460|p877] minimum: a turning point or vertex on a graph whose y-coordinate is lower than points immediately to its left and right.
[23461|p877] mixed number: a number with a whole number part and a fraction part.
[23462|p877] modal class: for grouped data, a class that has the highest frequency.
[23463|p877] mode: an average, the most frequently occurring value in a set of data.
[23464|p877] multiple: the result of multiplying a number by an integer.
[23465|p877] mutually exclusive events: events that cannot happen at the same time.
[23466|p877] negative correlation: a trend, in bivariate data, where, as one value increases, the other value decreases.
[23467|p877] net: the plane shape formed from the faces of a solid when it is 'unfolded'.
[23468|p877] numerator: the number on the top of a fraction.
[23469|p877] numerical data: data that is in the form of numbers.
[23470|p877] opposite: a 'shorter' side of a right-angled triangle that is 'opposite' an angle other than the right angle.
[23471|p877] Outcomes: the possible results of an 'experiment'.
[23472|p877] parabola: the graph of a quadratic relationship.
[23473|p877] parallel (lines): lines that are the same distance apart along their length.
[23474|p877] percentile: the value of data at a specified position (the data must be arranged in increasing order).
[23475|p877] perpendicular: at right angles to each other.
[23476|p877] perpendicular bisector: a line that cuts exactly through the middle of another line, making an angle of [EQ] with it.
[23477|p877] plane symmetry: a flat surface that cuts a solid into two halves so that one half is the mirror image of the other.
[23478|p877] positive correlation: a trend in bivariate data where as one value increase, so does the other.
[23479|p877] power: another word for exponent or index, indicating how many times a base number is multiplied by itself.
[23480|p877] prime factor: a prime number that divides exactly into another number with no remainder.
[23481|p877] prime number: a whole number greater than 1 which has only two factors: the number itself and 1.
[23482|p877] principal: the initial amount of money invested or borrowed.
[23483|p877] probability: a measure of how likely an event is to happen.
[23484|p877] profit: when goods are sold for more than they were bought, the profit is the selling price less the cost price.
[23485|p877] projection: the image of a line on a plane such that the angle between the angle and the image is the smallest possible.
[23486|p877] quadratic equation: an equation that contains a quadratic expression.
[23487|p877] quadratic expression: an expression where one term has a variable squared (and no variable with a higher power).
[23488|p877] Qualitative data: another name for categorical data.
[23489|p877] Quantitative data: another name for numerical data.
[23490|p877] Range: a measure of the spread of data. The difference between the highest value and the lowest value.
[23491|p877] rate: a comparison of two different quantities.
[23492|p877] Ratio: a comparison of amounts in a particular order.
[23493|p877] Rational number: a number that can be expressed as a fraction in the form [EQ] where [EQ] and [EQ] are integers and [EQ] .
[23494|p878] rationalising the denominator: expressing the denominator of any fraction as a rational number by removing any surds.
[23495|p878] reciprocal: the fraction obtained when the values of the numerator and denominator are interchanged. (See also hyperbola.)
[23496|p878] recurring decimal: a decimal that continues to infinity repeating itself at regular intervals.
[23497|p878] reflection: a transformation that creates an image by reflecting points in a given line.
[23498|p878] region: a region in a plane that satisfies a set of linear inequalities.
[23499|p878] relative frequency: the experimental probability of an event happening.
[23500|p878] rotation: a transformation that creates an image by rotating points by a given angle about a fixed point.
[23501|p878] rotational symmetry: symmetry by turning a shape about a fixed point so that it looks the same in different positions.
[23502|p878] sample space: all the possible outcomes of an event or combination of events.
[23503|p878] sample space diagram: a list or diagram that shows all the equally likely outcomes of an 'experiment'.
[23504|p878] scalar: a quantity that has size (magnitude) but not a direction.
[23505|p878] scale: a ratio that indicates how much smaller (or larger) a drawing is from the original object.
[23506|p878] scale factor: the multiplying factor for the sides of a shape that is enlarged from an original.
[23507|p878] scatter diagram: a diagram that plots pairs of bivariate data to help determine if there is any correlation between them.
[23508|p878] selling price: the price that a trader sells goods for.
[23509|p878] sequence: a number pattern or list of numbers in a particular order.
[23510|p878] set: a list or collection of objects that share a characteristic.
[23511|p878] set builder notation: a way of describing what elements are in a set without having to list them all.
[23512|p878] similar: plane objects that have the same shape and proportion but are different in size.
[23513|p878] simple interest: interest that is calculated only on the original amount borrowed or invested.
[23514|p878] simplest form: an equivalent fraction where the numerator and denominator are the smallest allowable whole numbers. Also called lowest terms.
[23515|p878] simultaneous: at the same time (or in the same position).
[23516|p878] sine ratio: for a given angle (other than the right-angle) in a right-angled triangle, the sine ratio is the length of the side opposite to the angle divided by the hypotenuse of the triangle.
[23517|p878] sine rule: in any triangle, the ratio of the sine of an angle to the length of the side opposite the angle is always the same.
[23518|p878] slant height: in a cone, the slant height is the shortest distance from a point on the circumference of the base to the apex.
[23519|p878] solution: the value obtained from solving an equation.
[23520|p878] speed: a rate that compares distance travelled in a given time.
[23521|p878] square number: the product obtained when an integer is multiplied by itself.
[23522|p878] square root: a number that, when multiplied by itself, gives a square.
[23523|p878] standard form: a shorthand method of writing very large or very small numbers.
[23524|p878] stationary point: a point on a graph where it stops increasing or decreasing.
[23525|p878] stem-and-leaf diagram: a type of bar graph made by arranging numerical data in a display, the first part of the numbers forms the stem and the last part forms the leaves.
[23526|p878] subject: the variable written by itself (usually to the left of the [EQ] sign).
[23527|p878] subscript notation: a method of writing the [EQ] term as [EQ] [EQ]
[23528|p878] subset: a set whose elements are all also members of another set.
[23529|p878] subtended angle: an angle formed at the meeting of two given lines.
[23530|p878] supplementary angles: two angles that add to give [EQ]
[23531|p878] surd: the irrational root of a number, for example, [EQ] .
[23532|p878] surface area: the total area of the faces of a three-dimensional solid.
[23533|p878] tangent ratio: for a given angle (other than the right-angle) in a right-angled triangle, the tangent ratio is the length of the side opposite to the angle divided by the side adjacent to the angle.
[23534|p878] term (in algebra): part of an expression.
[23535|p878] term (in sequences): any of the numbers in a sequence.
[23536|p878] terminating decimal: any decimal that has a fixed number of decimal places.
[23537|p878] theoretical probability: the chance of an event happening, calculated if it is known that the possible outcomes are equally likely.
[23538|p879] transformation: a change in the position of a point or line following a given rule.
[23539|p879] translation: a transformation that creates an image of a point by sliding' it along a plane.
[23540|p879] trial: an 'experiment' to determine the value of an outcome.
[23541|p879] turning point: a point on a graph where it changes direction. Usually a maximum or minimum point.
[23542|p879] two-way table: a table that summarises the data from two or more sets of data.
[23543|p879] union: the union of two sets is the set of all the elements in the combined sets.
[23544|p879] universal set: the set that contains all the elements you are dealing with.
[23545|p879] Upper bound: the largest value that a number (given to a specified accuracy) could be.
[23546|p879] upper quartile: the value of data at the [EQ] percentile.
[23547|p879] variable: a letter in a formula or equation that can have different values.
[23548|p879] vector: a quantity that has direction as well as size.
[23549|p879] Venn diagram: a pictorial method for illustrating the elements and interconnections of sets.
[23550|p879] vertically opposite: a pair of equal angles formed when two straight lines cross.
[23551|p879] vertices: the points where two or more edges of a plane shape meet.
[23552|p879] volume: the amount of space contained inside, or occupied by a solid object.
[23553|p879] Zero correlation: no apparent linear relationship between two sets of data.
